Refuting the Metempsychosis and Void Arguments
Refutations of metempsychosis and void arguments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the charges of metempsychosis and the void argument are rebutted in this passage.

Core Idea
The charge of metempsychosis is avoided by clarifying that souls go to a new world, not back to this one.

This part of the text tackles two more of 'Abbad's sub-arguments: that the proposed scenario implies metempsychosis, and that a void would exist between two worlds. The responses show that metempsychosis only applies if souls return to bodies in this world, and that the void argument relies on unproven premises about the sphere's shape and simplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the metempsychosis reply
Read the section on metempsychosis. Notice that the negation of the second possibility is not necessary; the charge of metempsychosis is refuted because the return is to another world, not to another body in this world.
Metempsychosis (tanāsukh) is specific to re-embodiment in this world.
2
Analyze the void argument
Read the void argument and the response. The response questions the premises: that the sphere is simple, that its shape is spherical, and that another world must have a sphere.
By denying these, the void hypothesis becomes unnecessary.
3
Compare the two refutations
Note how both arguments rely on assumptions about the nature of the world that are not conceded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : فلان) الأفلاك لا تقبل الخرق أجاب عنه في شرح المقاصد بأن إفناء هذا العالم بالكلية وايجاد عالم آخر فيه الجنة والنار لا يستلزم الخرق ولا غيره من المحالات، وأنت خبير بأن ما لايقبل الخرق وهو فساد من بعض الوجوه لا يقبل الفساد أصلاً وإن اختلف جهة الحكمين، فليتأمل. قوله فلا تخالطهما شيء من الكائنات الظاهر أن معنى المخالطة المنفية الدخول والوصول إليه لا ما ذكره الشارح إذ لا ينفي أن يكون في ثخن فلك التدوير فإن كونه وفساده لا يضر انتفاءه فيما فيه لكن لفظ الفاسدات لا يخلو عن تأييد لما ذكره الشارح فتدبر. قوله : ( وأنتم لا تقولون (به عدم القول به لا يختص بنفي الثاني إذ التناسخ يعم التعلق ببدن آخر في السماء أيضاً بل يبدن آخر على الإطلاق فإن تم كفى في تقرير الشبهة. قوله: (فينفرض بينهما خلاء فإن قلت لعل مالئاً آخر ملاه. قلت: هم يقولون : المالئ عالم آخر، فيكون كروياً أيضاً ، نعم يمكن أن يقال : لا نسلم أن الفلك بسيط ولا نسلم أن شكله الكرة ولا نسلم أنه يجب أن يكون للعالم الآخر فلك آخر . قوله : ( وأنت خبير بأن هذا دليل لمن ينكر وجودهما مطلقاً كما صرح به الإمام في الملخص، فالأولى هاهنا ما ذكره الآمدي وهو أن أفعال الله تعالى لا تخلو عن حكمة وفائدة، والفائدة في خلق الجنة والنار المجازاة بالثواب والعقاب وذلك غير مستحق قبل يوم القيامة إجماعاً من المسلمين فلا فائدة في خلقهما الآن فيكون ممتنعاً عقلاً، والجواب منع وجوب رعاية الحكمة في أفعاله تعالى وعلى تقدير تسليمه لا نسلم انحصار الفائدة في المجازاة فلعل فيه فائدة أخرى قد استأثر الله تعالى بعلمها إذ لا بعد في ذلك . أن نسلم امتناع الخرق على الأفلاك وقد تكلمنا على مأخذه ولا نسلم أنه في عالم العناصر قول بالتناسخ، وإنما يكون كذلك لو قلنا بإعادتها في أبدان آخر، ولا نسلم وجود عالم آخر محال وقد تكلمنا على ذلك فلا نعیده
His statement: 'because the spheres do not accept breach' — he answered it in Sharh al-Maqāsid that the annihilation of this world entirely and the creation of another world containing Paradise and Hell does not necessitate breach or any other impossibility, and you are aware that what does not accept breach — which is corruption in some respects — does not accept corruption at all, even if the aspect of the judgments differs, so let it be pondered. His statement: 'nothing of the things intermixes with them' — apparently the meaning of the negated intermixture is entry and arrival, not what the commentator mentioned, since it does not negate being in the thickness of the sphere of revolution, for its being and its corruption do not harm its non-existence in it, but the word 'corruptible things' is not free from supporting what the commentator mentioned, so reflect. His statement: 'and you do not affirm it' — the non-affirmation of it is not specific to negating the second [possibility], since metempsychosis includes attachment to another body in the sky as well, indeed to another body absolutely, so if it is established, it suffices for establishing the doubt. His statement: 'then between them a void would be assumed' — if you say: perhaps another filler filled it, I say: they say the filler is another world, so it would also be spherical; yes, it can be said: we do not concede that the sphere is simple, nor that its shape is spherical, nor that there must be another sphere for the other world. His statement: 'and you are aware that this proof is for one who denies their existence absolutely' — as the Imam stated in al-Mulakhkhaṣ, so the better course here is what al-Āmidī mentioned, which is that the actions of Allah the Exalted are not devoid of wisdom and benefit, and the benefit in creating Paradise and Hell is recompense with reward and punishment, and that is not due before the Day of Resurrection by consensus of Muslims, so there is no benefit in creating them now, thus it is rationally impossible. The answer: denial of the obligation of observing wisdom in His actions, and assuming its concession, we do not concede the confinement of benefit to recompense, for perhaps there is another benefit which Allah the Exalted has kept to Himself, for that is not far-fetched. That we concede the impossibility of breach on the spheres — we have spoken on its basis — and we do not concede that it is metempsychosis, it would be so only if we said their return in other bodies, and we do not concede that the existence of another world is impossible, and we have spoken on that so we do not repeat.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage provides detailed refutations of 'Abbad's sub-arguments. First, regarding the spheres not accepting breach: the answer from Sharh al-Maqāsid is that God could annihilate this world and create another containing Paradise and Hell without needing to breach the spheres. Second, the meaning of 'intermixture' is clarified as entry, not being in the sphere's thickness. Third, the charge of metempsychosis is addressed: it would apply only if souls return to another body in this world, not to a new world. Fourth, the void argument is countered by denying the premise that the sphere is simple or that another world must be spherical. Fifth, al-Āmidi's alternative reasoning is presented: God's actions require benefit, but benefit now is absent, so creation now is impossible; the answer is that wisdom is not obligatory on God, and there may be other benefits unknown to us. Finally, the text reiterates that the impossibility of breach, the charge of metempsychosis, and the impossibility of another world are not conceded.

Key Terms
التناسخ
الخلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If souls leave this world to another, that is metempsychosis.
Correction: Metempsychosis requires return to a body in this world; going to a new world is different.

Checkpoint
How does the passage refute the void argument?
A) It says the filler is Paradise and Hell.
B) It denies that the sphere is simple or spherical.
C) It says the void is filled by air.
D) It accepts the void but says it's not problematic.

Key Takeaway
The charges of metempsychosis and void are rebutted by clarifying that the scenario does not entail metempsychosis and by denying the premises of the void argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage refutes the idea that souls return to bodies in this world, affirming they go to a new world (the Hereafter). How does this truth confront your attachment to this world? What specific attachment are you unwilling to let go of, and what would it take for you to release it today?
?
The void argument was based on assumptions about the sphere's simplicity and shape that were not conceded. What assumptions about your own life—things you take for granted about your purpose, your time, or your success—might be false, and how would your life change if you examined them critically?
?
If you truly believed that every moment of this life is your only chance before an eternal new world, what would you start doing tomorrow that you are currently avoiding? What is one concrete step you will take this week to live with that reality?

Al-Āmidī's Alternative and Its Rebuttal
Al-Āmidī's alternative argument and its rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Āmidī's alternative argument that creation now is impossible due to lack of benefit, and how it is rebutted.

Core Idea
Benefit is not necessary for God's actions; there may be purposes only He knows.

The final part of the passage presents al-Āmidī's alternative to 'Abbad's proof: since the benefit of Paradise and Hell is recompense, and that is not due until Judgment Day, creating them now would lack benefit and thus be impossible. The response challenges the obligation of wisdom in God's actions and suggests there might be other benefits. This lesson examines this exchange.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Āmidī's argument
Read al-Āmidī's reasoning: God's actions must have benefit; the benefit of Paradise and Hell is recompense; recompense is not due now; therefore, creating them now is impossible.
This is based on the principle of wisdom (ḥikma).
2
Analyze the first rebuttal
The response denies that wisdom is obligatory on God. Even if conceded, benefit is not limited to recompense; there could be unknown benefits.
This rebuts the necessity of benefit.
3
Note the summary point
The passage concludes by reaffirming that the impossibility of breach, metempsychosis, and another world are not conceded.
This ties back to the earlier refutations.

Abu Hashim's First Proof: Perpetuity vs. Perishing
Abu Hashim's first proof and the three-fold answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's first argument that the perpetuity of Paradise's fruit contradicts the verse that everything perishes, and the three theological responses that reconcile them.

Core Idea
Paradise's perpetuity does not require individual items to last forever; they are perpetually replaced.

Abu Hashim challenges the doctrine that Paradise and Hell are already created by pointing to a seeming contradiction between two Quranic verses. The response shows that 'perpetual' can mean perpetual replacement or essential perpetuity, not numerical identity of each part. This lesson unpacks the first argument and its three-fold answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: a proof followed by three distinct answers.
The Arabic uses parentheses; the translation clarifies.
2
Understand the proof's logic
Identify why Abu Hashim sees a contradiction: if Paradise is already created, its fruit would have to perish like everything else, contradicting its description as perpetual.
The key terms are 'dāʾim' (perpetual) and 'hālik' (perishing).
3
Examine the three answers
Read each of the three responses carefully. The first uses replacement (badal), the second reinterprets 'perishing' as inherent perishability, the third posits a momentary annihilation and return.
Each answer preserves the perpetuity of Paradise in a different way.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these answers defend the doctrine that Paradise and Hell are already created. Ask yourself which answer seems strongest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج أبو هاشم بوجهين: الأول : قوله تعالى ( في وصف الجنة ( أكلها ) أي مأكولها ( دائم مع قوله : كل شيء ) أي موجود (هالك إلا وجهه فلو كانت ( الجنة ( مخلوقة وجب هلاك أكلها لا اندراجه، حينئذ فيما حكم عليه بالهلاك فلم يكن دائماً ) وهو باطل بالآية الأولى فتعين أنها ليست مخلوقة الآن فكذا النار ، ( الجواب : أكلها دائم بدلاً أي كلما فني منه شيء جيء ببدله فإن دوام أكل بعينه غير متصور ، لأنه إذا أكل فقد فني ) وذلك أي دوام أكله على سبيل البدل ( لا ينافي هلاكه أو نقول : المراد ) بهلاك كل شيء ( أنه هالك في حد ذاته لضعف الوجود الإمكاني فالتحق بالهالك المعدوم، أو نقول : إنهما أي الجنة والنار (تعدمان (آناً بتفريق الأجزاء دون إعدامها (ثم تعودان) بجمعها وذلك كاف في هلاكهما فتكونان دائمتين ذاتاً هالكتين صورة في آن. ( الثاني : قوله تعالى ) في وصف الجنة أيضاً ) عرضها السماوات والأرض ولا يتصور ذلك إلا بعد فناء السماوات والأرض لامتناع تداخل الأجسام الجواب : المراد أنها ) أي عرضها ( كعرض السماوات والأرض لامتناع أن يكون عرضها عرضهما بعينه لا حال البقاء ولا بعد (الفناء، إذ يمتنع قيام عرض واحد شخصي بمحلين موجودين معاً أو أحدهما موجود والآخر معدوم، وللتصريح في آية أخرى بأن عرضها كعرض السموات والأرض فيحمل هذا على تلك كما يقال : أبو يوسف أبو حنيفة ) أي مثله .
Abu Hashim argued with two proofs. The first: His statement (describing Paradise) 'its fruit' meaning its eatables 'is perpetual' along with His statement 'Everything' meaning every existent 'is perishing except His Face.' So if Paradise were created, its fruit would necessarily perish due to its inclusion under what is judged to perish, so it would not be perpetual. This is invalid due to the first verse, so it is determined that it is not created now, and likewise the Fire. The answer: Its fruit is perpetual by replacement, i.e., whenever something of it perishes, its replacement comes, for the perpetuity of a particular fruit is inconceivable, since if it is eaten it has perished. That is, its perpetuity by way of replacement does not contradict its perishing. Or we say: The meaning of 'everything perishes' is that it is perishing in its essence due to the weakness of contingent existence, so it joins the perished non-existent. Or we say: They (Paradise and Fire) are annihilated in an instant by separation of parts without their essences being annihilated, then they return by gathering them, and that suffices for their perishing, so they are perpetual in essence, perishing in form at an instant. The second: His statement (also describing Paradise) 'its width is the heavens and the earth,' and that is conceivable only after the annihilation of the heavens and the earth due to the impossibility of interpenetration of bodies. The answer: The intended meaning is that its width is like the width of the heavens and the earth, due to the impossibility that its width be identical with their width, neither during their existence nor after annihilation, since it is impossible for one individual accident to subsist in two loci both existing, or one existing and the other non-existent. And because in another verse it is explicitly stated that its width is like the width of the heavens and the earth, so this [verse] is interpreted in light of that, just as it is said: 'Abu Yusuf is Abu Hanifa,' i.e., his like.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage presents Abu Hashim's two Quranic proofs and their refutations. The first proof: the verse that the fruit of Paradise is perpetual (dāʾim) seems to contradict the verse that everything perishes (hālik). If Paradise were already created, its fruit would have to perish. The answer offers three interpretations: (1) the fruit is perpetual by replacement, not individual perpetuity; (2) 'everything perishes' means everything is inherently perishable due to contingency, not actual annihilation; (3) Paradise and Hell cease to exist momentarily by separation and then return, maintaining essential perpetuity. The second proof: the verse that the width of Paradise is like the heavens and the earth implies it must be after their annihilation, otherwise interpenetration would occur. The answer is that the verse means its width is comparable, not identical, as clarified by another verse where it says 'like the width,' so it is a simile (tashbīh) similar to saying 'Abu Yusuf is Abu Hanifa' meaning 'like him.'

Key Terms
الدائم (al-dāʾim)
الهالك (al-hālik)
البدل (al-badal)
الإمكاني (al-imkānī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'perpetual' (dāʾim) in the Quran must mean that each individual item lasts forever without change.
Correction: Perpetual can refer to the continuity of the supply or essence, not the numerical identity of individual items.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three answers given to Abu Hashim's first proof?
A) Perpetuity through replacement
B) Perishing means inherent perishability
C) Paradise and Hell are not yet created
D) Momentary annihilation of form

Key Takeaway
The perpetuity of Paradise is compatible with the perishing of individual items through replacement, inherent perishability, or momentary annihilation of form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Paradise, do you picture yourself enjoying the same pleasures forever? This lesson says the fruit is perpetually replaced, not the same fruit eternally. Does that change how you value the blessings of Paradise? What does that teach you about the nature of divine generosity?
?
The verse 'Everything perishes except His Face' is often recited. Do you truly believe that everything you cling to – your health, wealth, relationships, even your own body – is inherently perishable? What would change if you lived today with that reality at the front of your mind?
?
One answer says Paradise and Hell are annihilated in form for an instant then return. If your deeds will be presented in that instantaneous moment, what are you doing right now to ensure your good deeds are not lost? Will you act today as if your deeds could vanish in that moment?

Abu Hashim's Second Proof: The Width of Paradise
Abu Hashim's second proof and its answer via simile

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's second argument based on the verse about Paradise's width, and the response that it is a simile, not a literal identity.

Core Idea
The width of Paradise is compared to the heavens and earth, not identical – a common figure of speech.

The second proof concerns the verse describing Paradise's width as the heavens and earth, which seems to require that Paradise be created after their annihilation. The response clarifies that the verse uses a simile, as shown by another explicit verse. This lesson explores the logical and textual grounds for this interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that the proof relies on the literal reading of the verse, and the answer offers an alternative interpretation.
Key term: 'tadākhul' (interpenetration).
2
Understand the proof's problem
Why does a literal reading lead to a problem? Because if Paradise's width equals the heavens and earth now, they would interpenetrate, which is impossible.
Thus, the verse must be interpreted after the annihilation of the heavens and earth.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer gives two reasons: (1) logical impossibility of one accident subsisting in two loci, (2) another verse uses 'like' (ka), making it a simile.
The example 'Abu Yusuf is Abu Hanifa' illustrates a common simile.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider how the Ash'ari scholars use both logical reasoning and cross-referencing Quranic verses to interpret ambiguous texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج أبو هاشم بوجهين: الأول : قوله تعالى ( في وصف الجنة ( أكلها ) أي مأكولها ( دائم مع قوله : كل شيء ) أي موجود (هالك إلا وجهه فلو كانت ( الجنة ( مخلوقة وجب هلاك أكلها لا اندراجه، حينئذ فيما حكم عليه بالهلاك فلم يكن دائماً ) وهو باطل بالآية الأولى فتعين أنها ليست مخلوقة الآن فكذا النار ، ( الجواب : أكلها دائم بدلاً أي كلما فني منه شيء جيء ببدله فإن دوام أكل بعينه غير متصور ، لأنه إذا أكل فقد فني ) وذلك أي دوام أكله على سبيل البدل ( لا ينافي هلاكه أو نقول : المراد ) بهلاك كل شيء ( أنه هالك في حد ذاته لضعف الوجود الإمكاني فالتحق بالهالك المعدوم، أو نقول : إنهما أي الجنة والنار (تعدمان (آناً بتفريق الأجزاء دون إعدامها (ثم تعودان) بجمعها وذلك كاف في هلاكهما فتكونان دائمتين ذاتاً هالكتين صورة في آن. ( الثاني : قوله تعالى ) في وصف الجنة أيضاً ) عرضها السماوات والأرض ولا يتصور ذلك إلا بعد فناء السماوات والأرض لامتناع تداخل الأجسام الجواب : المراد أنها ) أي عرضها ( كعرض السماوات والأرض لامتناع أن يكون عرضها عرضهما بعينه لا حال البقاء ولا بعد (الفناء، إذ يمتنع قيام عرض واحد شخصي بمحلين موجودين معاً أو أحدهما موجود والآخر معدوم، وللتصريح في آية أخرى بأن عرضها كعرض السموات والأرض فيحمل هذا على تلك كما يقال : أبو يوسف أبو حنيفة ) أي مثله .
Abu Hashim argued with two proofs. The first: His statement (describing Paradise) 'its fruit' meaning its eatables 'is perpetual' along with His statement 'Everything' meaning every existent 'is perishing except His Face.' So if Paradise were created, its fruit would necessarily perish due to its inclusion under what is judged to perish, so it would not be perpetual. This is invalid due to the first verse, so it is determined that it is not created now, and likewise the Fire. The answer: Its fruit is perpetual by replacement, i.e., whenever something of it perishes, its replacement comes, for the perpetuity of a particular fruit is inconceivable, since if it is eaten it has perished. That is, its perpetuity by way of replacement does not contradict its perishing. Or we say: The meaning of 'everything perishes' is that it is perishing in its essence due to the weakness of contingent existence, so it joins the perished non-existent. Or we say: They (Paradise and Fire) are annihilated in an instant by separation of parts without their essences being annihilated, then they return by gathering them, and that suffices for their perishing, so they are perpetual in essence, perishing in form at an instant. The second: His statement (also describing Paradise) 'its width is the heavens and the earth,' and that is conceivable only after the annihilation of the heavens and the earth due to the impossibility of interpenetration of bodies. The answer: The intended meaning is that its width is like the width of the heavens and the earth, due to the impossibility that its width be identical with their width, neither during their existence nor after annihilation, since it is impossible for one individual accident to subsist in two loci both existing, or one existing and the other non-existent. And because in another verse it is explicitly stated that its width is like the width of the heavens and the earth, so this [verse] is interpreted in light of that, just as it is said: 'Abu Yusuf is Abu Hanifa,' i.e., his like.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage presents Abu Hashim's two Quranic proofs and their refutations. The first proof: the verse that the fruit of Paradise is perpetual (dāʾim) seems to contradict the verse that everything perishes (hālik). If Paradise were already created, its fruit would have to perish. The answer offers three interpretations: (1) the fruit is perpetual by replacement, not individual perpetuity; (2) 'everything perishes' means everything is inherently perishable due to contingency, not actual annihilation; (3) Paradise and Hell cease to exist momentarily by separation and then return, maintaining essential perpetuity. The second proof: the verse that the width of Paradise is like the heavens and the earth implies it must be after their annihilation, otherwise interpenetration would occur. The answer is that the verse means its width is comparable, not identical, as clarified by another verse where it says 'like the width,' so it is a simile (tashbīh) similar to saying 'Abu Yusuf is Abu Hanifa' meaning 'like him.'

Key Terms
التداخل (al-tadākhul)
التشبيه (al-tashbīh)
العرض (al-ʿarad, accident)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'its width is the heavens and the earth' must be taken literally, implying Paradise is as large as the entire cosmos.
Correction: The verse uses a simile; Paradise's width is comparable but not identical to the heavens and earth.

Checkpoint
What Arabic particle is used in the other verse that clarifies the simile?
A) كَ (ka)
B) بِ (bi)
C) لِ (li)
D) مِ (mi)

Key Takeaway
The verse comparing Paradise's width to the heavens and earth is a simile, as confirmed by another Quranic verse, so it does not imply Paradise is uncreated now.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'its width is the heavens and the earth', did you ever assume it was a literal measurement? How does Abu Hashim's error challenge you to approach Allah's words with humility and seek scholarly explanation instead of relying on your own understanding?
?
You have just learned that a single accident cannot occupy two loci. How does this logical principle affect your interpretation of other verses describing divine attributes? Are there any attributes you may have misunderstood due to neglecting this principle?
?
The scholars resolved the apparent contradiction by using another verse. How often do you cross-reference Quranic verses before forming an opinion about a matter of faith? What will you change in your study habits after today's lesson to avoid falling into similar mistakes?

Paradise's Perpetuity: Objection and Response
Abu Hashim argues from the permanence of paradise's fruits and the verse about everything perishing, and answers are given.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's argument that paradise must not yet be created and the Ash'ari responses that reconcile its perpetuity with the Qur'anic statement that all things perish.

Core Idea
Paradise's fruit is perpetual by replacement, not by permanent existence.

Abu Hashim, a leading Mu'tazilite, raised a clever objection: if paradise were already created, its fruit would be subject to perishing according to the verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face.' Yet the Qur'an says paradise's fruit is perpetual. So paradise must not be created yet. The Ash'ari response involves multiple nuanced explanations, preserving both the perpetuity of paradise and the universality of perishing.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part of the passage where Abu Hashim presents his argument using the two verses. Note the logical deduction: if paradise were created, its fruit would be included in 'everything' that perishes, contradicting its perpetuity.
The objection is based on a literal reading of 'كل شيء هالك'.
2
First answer: perpetuity by replacement
The first response explains that the fruit's perpetuity is through replacement (بدلاً), not by the same fruit persisting. When a fruit is eaten, a new one replaces it, so the overall fruit supply is perpetual even though individual fruits perish.
This answer directly addresses the conflict between the two verses.
3
Second answer: perishing as essential weakness
The second response reinterprets 'perishing' to mean the inherent weakness of contingent existence, not literal annihilation. In this sense, everything is 'perishing' in itself, but Paradise remains.
This interpretation avoids claiming that Paradise suffers annihilation.
4
Third answer: momentary dispersion and return
The third response suggests that Paradise and Hell perish for an instant by dispersion of parts and then are restored. This satisfies the universality of perishing while allowing them to be perpetual in essence.
This is a more speculative answer, but it preserves both verses.
5
Reflect on the marginal note
The passage ends with a note clarifying an alternative explanation and why it was rejected. Read this to see how scholars carefully considered all possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج أبو هاشم بوجهين: الأول : قوله تعالى ( في وصف الجنة ( أكلها ) أي مأكولها ( دائم مع قوله : كل شيء ) أي موجود (هالك إلا وجهه فلو كانت ( الجنة ( مخلوقة وجب هلاك أكلها لا اندراجه، حينئذ فيما حكم عليه بالهلاك فلم يكن دائماً ) وهو باطل بالآية الأولى فتعين أنها ليست مخلوقة الآن فكذا النار ، ( الجواب : أكلها دائم بدلاً أي كلما فني منه شيء جيء ببدله فإن دوام أكل بعينه غير متصور ، لأنه إذا أكل فقد فني ) وذلك أي دوام أكله على سبيل البدل ( لا ينافي هلاكه أو نقول : المراد ) بهلاك كل شيء ( أنه هالك في حد ذاته لضعف الوجود الإمكاني فالتحق بالهالك المعدوم، أو نقول : إنهما أي الجنة والنار (تعدمان (آناً بتفريق الأجزاء دون إعدامها (ثم تعودان) بجمعها وذلك كاف في هلاكهما فتكونان دائمتين ذاتاً هالكتين صورة في آن. قوله: (وجب هلاك (أكلها يجوز أن يقال : فليكن أكلها يوم الجزاء وذاتها الآن لكن لما ورد على بقاء ذاتها أيضاً قوله تعالى: كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه ) [ القصص : ٨٨] لم يلتفت إلى هذا القول وأجيب بما يقطع دار الشبهة.
Abu Hashim argued with two aspects: The first: His statement the Exalted (in describing Paradise: 'its fruit' i.e., its edible part 'is perpetual' along with His statement: 'Everything' i.e., existing 'is perishing except His Face'. So if Paradise were created, its fruit would necessarily perish due to its inclusion in that which was judged to perish, so it would not be perpetual.) And it is invalidated by the first verse, so it is established that it is not created now, and likewise the Fire. (The answer: Its fruit is perpetual by replacement, i.e., whenever something from it perishes, a replacement is brought; indeed, the perpetuity of a specific fruit is inconceivable because when eaten it has perished.) And that (i.e., its perpetuity by replacement) does not negate its perishing. Or we say: The intended meaning of the perishing of everything is that it is perishing in itself due to the weakness of contingent existence, so it joins the perished nonexistent. Or we say: They (i.e., Paradise and the Fire) perish for an instant by dispersion of parts without annihilation, then return by their gathering, and that suffices for their perishing, so they are perpetual in essence, perishing in form in an instant. (His saying: 'The perishing of its fruit' it may be said: Let its fruit be on the Day of Requital and its essence now, but since there also came regarding the permanence of its essence the statement of the Exalted: 'Everything is perishing except His Face' [Qasas 28:88], this statement was not considered, and it was answered in a way that cuts the root of the doubt.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the Mu'tazilite scholar Abu Hashim and the Ash'ari response. The objection uses two verses: one stating that Paradise's fruit is perpetual, and another stating that everything except God will perish. The Mu'tazilites argue that if Paradise were already created, its fruit would be subject to perishing, contradicting its perpetuity. Therefore, Paradise must not be created yet. The Ash'ari answer provides three possible explanations: (1) The perpetuity of the fruit is by replacement, not by the same fruit persisting; (2) The perishing in the verse refers to the essential weakness of contingent beings, not literal annihilation; (3) Paradise and Hell perish momentarily by dispersion of parts and are then restored, so they are both perpetual in essence and perishing in form. A marginal note adds a further clarification: one might say the fruit exists on the Day of Requital while the essence exists now, but the verse about all things perishing applies to the essence as well, so this explanation is rejected and a decisive answer is given.

Key Terms
دائم بدلاً
الوجود الإمكاني
تفريق الأجزاء
خرق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Mu'tazilite argument proves paradise is uncreated.
Correction: The response shows that the perpetuity of fruit does not require paradise to be uncreated; it can be perpetual by replacement or the verse's meaning is reinterpreted.

Checkpoint
What is the first of the three answers given to Abu Hashim's argument regarding the perpetuity of paradise's fruit?
A) The fruit is perpetual in itself and not subject to perishing.
B) The fruit is perpetual by replacement, so its perpetuity is not contradicted by perishing.
C) The fruit will be created only on the Day of Requital.
D) The verse about perishing does not apply to Paradise.

Key Takeaway
The perpetuity of paradise is not contradicted by the general perishing of all things because perpetuity can be by replacement, perishing can refer to contingent weakness, or paradise can momentarily perish and be restored.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson shows that the fruit of Paradise is perpetual by replacement, not by individual permanence. Are you clinging to temporary 'fruits' in this life—status, relationships, achievements—as if they must last forever? What would it mean for you to seek the perpetual only from Allah, who alone remains unchanged?
?
The third response states that Paradise and Hell perish for an instant and then return. This teaches that even the most permanent created things depend on Allah's will for every moment. In your daily life, do you act as if your blessings are self-sustaining, or do you consciously recognize that they could vanish in an instant without His preservation? What habit could you adopt to remind yourself of this daily?
?
Abu Hashim's argument assumed that 'everything perishes' applies uniformly. The Ash'ari answers show nuance: perishing can mean different things. Is there a belief you hold about Allah—maybe about His mercy or justice—that you treat as black-and-white without considering deeper scriptural nuances? Will you commit today to seeking knowledge to refine your understanding, rather than affirming shortcuts?

Abu Hashim's Second Argument and Its Refutation
Abu Hashim argues from scriptural description; Ash'ari answers via comparison.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari school refutes the Mu'tazilite argument that Paradise's description implies it is not yet created, by interpreting the verse as a comparison rather than an identity.

Core Idea
Paradise's width is a comparison, not an identity.

The Mu'tazilites argue that if Paradise's width is as the heavens and earth, it must be enormous and not yet created, since the heavens and earth still exist. The Ash'ari response clarifies that the verse is a comparison of magnitude, not a statement of identical dimensions. This lesson examines that argument and its refutation, highlighting the role of rational interpretation in understanding scriptural descriptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own presentation come through.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite argument
Find the claim that Paradise's width being as the heavens and earth is inconceivable except after their annihilation, due to the impossibility of interpenetration of bodies.
This is the core of Abu Hashim's reasoning.
3
Understand the Ash'ari response
Notice that the answer does not deny the width but interprets it as a comparison (تشبيه). It explains that identical width is impossible because a single accident cannot exist in two loci.
The response also cites another verse that explicitly says 'like' (كعرض).
4
Reflect on the analogy
Consider the example 'Abu Yusuf is Abu Hanifa' meaning 'like him'. This illustrates how comparisons work in Arabic.
5
Consider the marginal note
The note adds a practical argument: if the width were identical, we would be in Paradise now—an absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : قوله تعالى ) في وصف الجنة أيضاً ) عرضها السماوات والأرض ولا يتصور ذلك إلا بعد فناء السماوات والأرض لامتناع تداخل الأجسام الجواب : المراد أنها ) أي عرضها ( كعرض السماوات والأرض لامتناع أن يكون عرضها عرضهما بعينه لا حال البقاء ولا بعد (الفناء، إذ يمتنع قيام عرض واحد شخصي بمحلين موجودين معاً أو أحدهما موجود والآخر معدوم، وللتصريح في آية أخرى بأن عرضها كعرض السموات والأرض فيحمل هذا على تلك كما يقال : أبو يوسف أبو حنيفة ) أي مثله . قوله : ( لامتناع تداخل الأجسام ولأنا لسنا الآن في الجنة بالاتفاق فلو كان السماء والأرض عرض الجنة الآن لكنا فيه الآن .
The second: His statement the Exalted (in describing Paradise also: 'its width is as the heavens and the earth' and this is inconceivable except after the annihilation of the heavens and the earth because of the impossibility of interpenetration of bodies. The answer: The intended meaning is that its width is like the width of the heavens and the earth, because it is impossible that its width is identical to their width, neither in the state of existence nor after (annihilation), since it is impossible for a single particular accident to subsist in two loci that exist together or one exists and the other is nonexistent. And because another verse explicitly states that its width is like the width of the heavens and the earth, so this [verse] is interpreted in light of that, just as it is said: 'Abu Yusuf is Abu Hanifa' i.e., like him. His saying: 'Because of the impossibility of interpenetration of bodies' and because we are not now in Paradise by consensus, so if the heaven and earth were the width of Paradise now, we would be in it now.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage presents Abu Hashim's second argument and the Ash'ari response. The Mu'tazilites argue that the description 'its width is as the heavens and the earth' implies Paradise must be enormous, but since the heavens and earth still exist, Paradise cannot yet be created (as it would require interpenetration). The answer explains that the verse is a comparison (تشبيه), not a statement of identity. The width is like that of the heavens and earth in magnitude, not literally the same dimensions. It is impossible for a single accident (such as a specific width) to exist in two places at once. Another verse clarifies it as 'like the width' (كعرض), so this verse is interpreted accordingly. A marginal note adds: we are not in Paradise now, so if the width were literally the same as the heavens and earth, we would be in it now—an impossibility.

Key Terms
تداخل الأجسام
عرض واحد شخصي
تشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'its width is as the heavens and the earth' must be taken literally to mean identical dimensions.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that it is a comparison of magnitude, not identity, as proven by rational impossibility of a single accident in two loci and by another verse explicitly using 'like'.

Checkpoint
How is the phrase 'its width is as the heavens and the earth' interpreted in the answer?
A) It means Paradise is literally as wide as the heavens and the earth.
B) It is a comparison meaning its width is like that of the heavens and the earth.
C) It refers to the width of Paradise after the heavens and earth are destroyed.
D) It indicates that Paradise currently occupies the same space as the heavens and earth.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response clarifies that scriptural comparisons must be understood as similitude to avoid rational absurdities, relying on both logical arguments and cross-referencing other verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of the Quran that seems to describe something physical about the afterlife, do you immediately accept it literally, or do you pause to consider whether it might be a comparison? What does your answer reveal about your trust in reason alongside revelation?
?
The Ash'ari response uses the impossibility of an accident existing in two places to reject a literal reading. Are there beliefs you hold about the unseen that, if examined rationally, might lead to contradictions? Are you willing to reinterpret them in light of clear principles?
?
If you were to apply the method of cross-referencing verses (as done here with 57:21 and 3:133) to other doctrines, might you have to change some of your current views? What would it cost you to do so?

The Mu'tazila/Khawarij First Scriptural Proof for Eternal Punishment
Mu'tazila/Khawarij cite three verses and argue 'khulud' means eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila/Khawarij argument from Qur'anic verses for eternal punishment and their linguistic claim about the word 'khulud'.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue that 'khulud' in the Qur'an always means eternal duration.

The Mu'tazila and Khawarij rely on transmitted evidence to argue that grave sinners will be eternally punished. In this lesson, we examine their first proof: three Qur'anic verses that seem to threaten eternal punishment. They also present a linguistic argument that 'khulud' literally means 'eternal duration,' not merely a long stay. Understanding this argument is crucial before we turn to the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the three verses cited
Read the Arabic of verses 2:81, 4:14, and 4:93, focusing on the word 'khulud' (eternal). Notice how each verse promises eternal punishment for certain actions.
These verses are central to the debate.
2
Understand the linguistic argument
The Mu'tazila claim that 'khulud' means perpetual duration. They support this by citing verse 21:34, which denies immortality to any human. They argue that if 'khulud' meant only a long stay, the verses would be false.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the Mu'tazila need to prove that 'khulud' means eternal. If it only meant a long stay, the verses would not prove eternal punishment, so their entire case rests on this linguistic claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستعانوا من النقل بوجهين. الأول بآيات تشعر بالخلود كقوله تعالى : ﴿من كسب سيئة وأحاطت به خطيئة فأولئك أصحاب النار هم فيها خالدون﴾ [البقرة : ۸۱]، وقوله : ﴿ومن يعص الله ورسوله ويتعد حدوده يدخله ناراً خالداً فيها﴾ [النساء : ١٤ ] ، وقوله : ﴿ومن يقتل مؤمناً متعمداً فجزاؤه جهنم خالداً فيها﴾ [النساء : ۹۳]، قالوا: والخلود حقيقة في الدوام لقوله تعالى: ﴿وما جعلنا لبشر من قبلك الخلد﴾ [الأنبياء : ٣٤] ، مع أنه تعالى قد جعل لكثير المكث الطويل، فلو حمل الخلود على المكث الطويل لم تصدق هذه الآيات . والجواب لا نسلم أن من له حسنات من الإيمان والطاعات فقد أحاطت به خطيئته من بعض جوانبه لا محيطة به ولا نسلم أن من اكتسب كبيرة فقد تعدى حدوده بل تعدى بعض حدوده والمراد بالآية الثالثة من قتل مؤمناً لأنه مؤمن ولا يكون ذلك القاتل إلا كافراً، فالآيات المذكورة لا تتناول صاحب الكبيرة سلمنا تناولها إياه لكن الخلود المذكور فيها هو المكث الطويل وما ذكرتم من الاستدلال على أنه حقيقة في الدوام معارض بما يقال في الاستعمال الشائع : مخلد ووقف مخلد وخلد الله ملكه والمراد طول المدة بلا شبهة فالأولى حينئذ أن يجعل حقيقة في المكث الطويل سواء كان معه دوام أولا ، احتراز عن لزوم المجاز أو الاشتراك والآية المذكورة حملناها على الدوام الذي هو أحد قسمي المكث الطويل لقرينة الحال، فلا يلزم مجاز لأن خصوصية ذلك القسم مستفادة من خارج لا مقصودة بنفس اللفظ
And they sought help from transmitted evidence in two ways. The first: verses that indicate eternity, such as His saying: 'Whoever earns evil and his sin encompasses him, those are the companions of the Fire, they will abide therein eternally' [al-Baqara 2:81], and His saying: 'And whoever disobeys God and His Messenger and transgresses His limits, He will admit him to a Fire, abiding eternally therein' [an-Nisa 4:14], and His saying: 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding eternally therein' [an-Nisa 4:93]. They said: 'Khulud' is literal for perpetuity, because God says: 'And We did not grant any human before you immortality' [al-Anbiya 21:34], despite that God has granted many a long stay. So if khulud were interpreted as long stay, these verses would not be true. The answer: We do not grant that the one who has good deeds of faith and obedience is encompassed by his sin from some sides, not fully encompassed; and we do not grant that the one who commits a major sin has transgressed His limits, rather he has transgressed some of His limits; and the intention of the third verse is the one who kills a believer because he is a believer, and such a killer is only a disbeliever. Thus the mentioned verses do not include the committer of a major sin. Even if we grant that they include him, the khulud mentioned therein is long stay, and what you mentioned of evidence that it is literal for perpetuity is countered by what is said in common usage: 'mukhalled' and 'waqf mukhalled' and 'khallada Allah mulkahu' meaning long duration without doubt. So it is better then to make it literal for long stay, whether with perpetuity or not, to avoid metaphor or homonymy. And the mentioned verse we have interpreted as perpetuity, which is one of the two divisions of long stay, due to the contextual evidence, so there is no necessity for metaphor because the specificity of that division is derived externally, not intended by the word itself.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila/Khawarij first scriptural argument for eternal punishment, citing three Qur'anic verses that appear to threaten eternal punishment for sinners. They argue that 'khulud' literally means eternal duration, citing the verse 'We did not grant any human before you immortality' to show that khulud implies perpetuity, and that interpreting it as a long stay would render the verses false. The response makes two points. First, it denies that the verses apply to the grave sinner: the verse about being encompassed by sin refers to unbelief, not to someone with good deeds; the verse about transgression of limits refers to complete transgression, not just a single sin; and the verse about killing a believer intentionally is interpreted as referring to a disbeliever. Second, even if the verses apply, 'khulud' is commonly used for long duration, not necessarily eternal, as seen in phrases like 'mukhalled' (perpetual endowment) and 'khallada Allah mulkahu' (may God perpetuate his kingdom). Therefore, it is better to interpret khulud as meaning long stay, and when it means eternal in some verses, that is due to contextual evidence, not the word's primary meaning.

دوام
المكث الطويل
الآيات

Which verse do the Mu'tazila cite to support their claim that 'khulud' means eternal duration?

B) 4:14
C) 21:34
D) 4:93

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila and Khawarij rely on three Qur'anic verses and the literal meaning of 'khulud' to argue for eternal punishment of grave sinners.

?

?
When you read the verses about 'khālidūn' in Hell, do you instinctively assume they apply to others, or do you fear for yourself? What does your answer reveal about how seriously you take your own sins?

The Refutation: Do the Verses Apply? And What Does 'Khulud' Really Mean?
The Ash'ari response denies applicability and reinterprets 'khulud' as long stay.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-part refutation of the Mu'tazila argument: first, the verses do not apply to the grave sinner, and second, 'khulud' primarily means long stay, not eternal.

Core Idea
The refutation shows that the verses are not about grave sinners and that 'khulud' is used for long duration in common Arabic.

Having presented the Mu'tazila claim, the author now offers a two-part response. First, he argues that the verses cited do not actually refer to a Muslim who commits a major sin; rather, they describe unbelief or complete transgression. Second, even if they did refer to such a person, the word 'khulud' in the Qur'an means 'long stay,' not necessarily eternal, as shown by common usage. This lesson unpacks these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Examine the first part of the refutation
The author denies that the verses apply. For verse 2:81, he says the 'encompassing sin' refers to unbelief, not a partial sin. For 4:14, he says 'transgresses His limits' means complete transgression, not just one sin. For 4:93, he says the killer is a disbeliever. So the verses do not include the grave sinner.
2
Examine the second part of the refutation
Even if the verses applied, the author argues that 'khulud' literally means 'long stay.' He gives examples from common usage: 'mukhalled' (perpetual endowment) and 'khallada Allah mulkahu' (may God perpetuate his kingdom) both imply long duration, not necessarily eternal. Therefore, it is better to interpret 'khulud' as long stay, and when it means eternal, that is due to context.
3
Reflect on the overall strategy
Notice how the refutation uses two independent lines: one denying the premise (the verses apply), and one denying the conclusion (khulud means eternal). This is a strong argumentative approach.

Second Scriptural Proof and Its Refutation
Presentation and refutation of the second scriptural proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second scriptural proof used by the Mu'tazila and Khawarij for eternal punishment, and how it is refuted by limiting the term 'fujjar' to disbelievers and interpreting the verse metaphorically.

Core Idea
The word 'fujjar' in the Qur'an specifically refers to complete disbelievers, not all sinners.

In this passage, the author presents the second scriptural argument from the Mu'tazila/Khawarij, who cite a verse from Surat al-Infitar to claim that all wicked people will remain in Hell forever. The response shows that the verse's wording is limited to disbelievers and that its apparent meaning cannot be taken literally. This lesson examines the proof and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and the proof
Read the verse from al-Infitar and the Mu'tazila argument that it proves eternal punishment for all wicked.
Notice how they infer: if they leave, they would be absent, contradicting the verse.
2
Study the first part of the answer
The term 'fujjar' is restricted to complete disbelievers, as shown by another verse (Abasa 80:42).
This limits the scope of the verse.
3
Study the second part of the answer
The apparent meaning would imply current punishment, which is false, so the verse must be interpreted as deserving punishment, not actual eternal punishment.
This shows the verse is metaphorical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني من الوجهين قوله تعالى : ﴿وإن الفجار لفي جحيم يصلونها يوم الدين وما هم عنها بغائبين﴾ [الانفطار : ٤١-١٥] ولو خرجوا عنها لكانوا غائبين عنها . الجواب : عن هذه الآية وحدها أن لفظ الفجار لا يتناول إلا من هو كامل في فجوره وهو الكافر كما يدل عليه قوله : ﴿أولئك هم الكفرة الفجرة﴾ [عبس: ٤٢] ، وأيضاً ظاهرها يقتضي كون الفجار في الجحيم في الحال ومعلوم أنه ليس الأمر كذلك فوجب التأويل باستحقاق النار وعدم غيبتهم عن حبس وقوله : (فلا يلزم مجاز فيه بحث لأن لام الخلد إما للماهية من حيث هي أو للعهد الذهني وأياً ما كان لا يصح النفي إلا بأن يجعل مجازاً عن فرد خاص، فتأمل.
The second of the two ways: His saying: 'Indeed the wicked will be in Hellfire, they will burn therein on the Day of Recompense, and they will not be absent from it' [al-Infitar 82:14-15]; if they were to leave it, they would be absent from it. The answer: concerning this verse alone, that the word 'fujjar' includes only one who is complete in his wickedness, and he is the disbeliever, as indicated by His saying: 'Those are the disbelievers, the wicked' [Abasa 80:42]. Also, its apparent meaning implies that the wicked are in Hellfire currently, and it is known that this is not the case, so interpretation is necessary: they deserve the Fire and are not absent from it—imprisonment. And his saying: so there is no necessity for metaphor—there is investigation in it, because the lam of khulud is either for the essence as such or for mental reference, and whichever it is, negation is not valid unless it is made metaphorical for a specific individual, so reflect.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage presents the second scriptural proof from the Mu'tazila/Khawarij, citing the verse from al-Infitar that states the wicked will be in Hellfire and will never be absent from it. The response argues that the word 'fujjar' (wicked) in this verse refers exclusively to those who are completely wicked, i.e., disbelievers, as indicated by another verse (Abasa 80:42) where 'fujjar' is equated with disbelievers. Additionally, the apparent meaning of the verse suggests that the wicked are currently in Hellfire, which is false, so the verse must be interpreted metaphorically: it means they deserve the Fire and will not be absent from it, possibly referring to imprisonment. The passage ends with a comment on a previous statement: the claim that 'no metaphor is necessary' is debated, because the particle 'lam' in 'khulud' (eternity) could be for the essence or for mental reference; in either case, negation of leaving would only be valid if taken as metaphor for a specific individual. This is a subtle grammatical and theological point.

Key Terms
الفجار
الجحيم
الغيبة
التأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Indeed the wicked will be in Hellfire...' proves that all sinners will be eternally punished.
Correction: The term 'fujjar' in this verse refers only to complete disbelievers, not all sinners, as clarified by another Qur'anic verse.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, who does the word 'fujjar' in the verse from al-Infitar refer to?
A) All sinners
B) Complete disbelievers
C) Muslims who commit sins
D) Hypocrites

Key Takeaway
The second scriptural proof is refuted by restricting 'fujjar' to disbelievers and interpreting the verse as metaphorical for deserving punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a Quranic verse, how often do you assume its apparent meaning is the whole truth without checking other verses or scholarly explanations? Is there a belief you hold that might be based on a partial understanding? Will you commit to studying this verse in context this week?
?
This lesson teaches that 'fujjar' refers only to complete disbelievers, not all sinners. Do you ever write off Muslims who commit major sins as if they were beyond Allah's mercy? How can you align your heart with the Quran's broader teaching that only unrepentant disbelief deserves eternal punishment?
?
The refutation here shows that even a seemingly clear verse must be interpreted when its literal meaning leads to a falsehood. Are there any theological beliefs you hold that, if taken literally, would contradict other truths of Islam? Will you humbly re-examine them in light of the full Qur'an and Sunnah?

The Subtle Point about Lam al-Khulud
Grammatical and theological analysis of the particle 'lam' in 'khulud'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical and theological debate about the particle 'lam' in 'khulud' (eternity) and its implications for interpreting scriptural proofs.

Core Idea
The particle 'lam' in 'khulud' can refer to essence or mental reference, affecting the validity of negation.

In concluding the discussion of the second scriptural proof, the author adds a subtle comment on a previous claim that 'no metaphor is necessary.' He points out that the particle 'lam' in the word 'khulud' (eternity) can be interpreted in two ways, each requiring careful consideration. This brief passage delves into Arabic grammar and logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being critiqued
The previous statement that metaphor is not needed is scrutinized.
2
Understand the two possible meanings of 'lam al-khulud'
It can be for the essence (māhiyya) or for mental reference (al-ʿahd al-dhihnī).
3
Realize the consequence
In both cases, negation of leaving would only be valid if the verse is taken metaphorically for a specific individual.
The author ends with 'ta'ammal' (reflect), indicating a deeper issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني من الوجهين قوله تعالى : ﴿وإن الفجار لفي جحيم يصلونها يوم الدين وما هم عنها بغائبين﴾ [الانفطار : ٤١-١٥] ولو خرجوا عنها لكانوا غائبين عنها . الجواب : عن هذه الآية وحدها أن لفظ الفجار لا يتناول إلا من هو كامل في فجوره وهو الكافر كما يدل عليه قوله : ﴿أولئك هم الكفرة الفجرة﴾ [عبس: ٤٢] ، وأيضاً ظاهرها يقتضي كون الفجار في الجحيم في الحال ومعلوم أنه ليس الأمر كذلك فوجب التأويل باستحقاق النار وعدم غيبتهم عن حبس وقوله : (فلا يلزم مجاز فيه بحث لأن لام الخلد إما للماهية من حيث هي أو للعهد الذهني وأياً ما كان لا يصح النفي إلا بأن يجعل مجازاً عن فرد خاص، فتأمل.
The second of the two ways: His saying: 'Indeed the wicked will be in Hellfire, they will burn therein on the Day of Recompense, and they will not be absent from it' [al-Infitar 82:14-15]; if they were to leave it, they would be absent from it. The answer: concerning this verse alone, that the word 'fujjar' includes only one who is complete in his wickedness, and he is the disbeliever, as indicated by His saying: 'Those are the disbelievers, the wicked' [Abasa 80:42]. Also, its apparent meaning implies that the wicked are in Hellfire currently, and it is known that this is not the case, so interpretation is necessary: they deserve the Fire and are not absent from it—imprisonment. And his saying: so there is no necessity for metaphor—there is investigation in it, because the lam of khulud is either for the essence as such or for mental reference, and whichever it is, negation is not valid unless it is made metaphorical for a specific individual, so reflect.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage presents the second scriptural proof from the Mu'tazila/Khawarij, citing the verse from al-Infitar that states the wicked will be in Hellfire and will never be absent from it. The response argues that the word 'fujjar' (wicked) in this verse refers exclusively to those who are completely wicked, i.e., disbelievers, as indicated by another verse (Abasa 80:42) where 'fujjar' is equated with disbelievers. Additionally, the apparent meaning of the verse suggests that the wicked are currently in Hellfire, which is false, so the verse must be interpreted metaphorically: it means they deserve the Fire and will not be absent from it, possibly referring to imprisonment. The passage ends with a comment on a previous statement: the claim that 'no metaphor is necessary' is debated, because the particle 'lam' in 'khulud' (eternity) could be for the essence or for mental reference; in either case, negation of leaving would only be valid if taken as metaphor for a specific individual. This is a subtle grammatical and theological point.

الماهية
العهد الذهني

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, for the negation in the verse to be valid without metaphor, what must be true about the lam of khulud?
A) It must be for the essence
B) It must be for mental reference
C) Neither option makes the negation valid without metaphor
D) It must be for a specific individual

Key Takeaway
The particle 'lam' in 'khulud' does not straightforwardly support a literal interpretation; both its possible grammatical functions require metaphorical interpretation to preserve the negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of the Qur'an that seems straightforward, do you ever pause to consider that even a single letter like 'lam' could carry layers of meaning that challenge your initial understanding? How willing are you to let such grammatical details unsettle your assumptions? 

This lesson shows that a seemingly simple particle can force a reconsideration of the entire interpretation. Will you commit to studying the intricacies of Qur'anic Arabic, even when it feels tedious, so that you may better grasp the intended meanings?
?
The author concludes with 'so reflect' (fa-tammal). When was the last time you truly reflected on a single word or letter in the Qur'an, not just reading past it but interrogating its grammatical function and implications? 

This kind of reflection is essential for deepening your iman. Before you finish this lesson, take a moment to think: what other parts of Allah's speech might you have glossed over without due consideration? Pledge to slow down and examine at least one difficult passage this week with the same scrutiny.

First Transmitted Objection on Eternal Punishment and Its Refutation
Mu'tazila cite three verses on khulud, argue for eternal punishment, and Ash'ari respond with reinterpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's first transmitted argument for the eternal punishment of major sinners and the Ash'ari response that these verses refer only to unbelievers.

Core Idea
The verses on eternal punishment apply only to unbelievers whose sin completely dominates them.

In this lesson, we examine the first of two transmitted proofs the Mu'tazila used to argue that major sinners will be eternally punished. They cite several Quranic verses that speak of 'khulud' (eternity) for sinners. The Ash'ari response carefully reinterprets these verses to show they refer to those whose sin encompasses them entirely — that is, unbelievers — not to believers who commit major sins.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila argument
Read the verses cited by the Mu'tazila and their reasoning that 'khulud' means eternal duration. Notice how they use the word 'khulud' in Quran 21:34 to support their interpretation.
They also mention that long stay is given to many, so khulud must be more than that.
2
Examine the Ash'ari response
The response denies that the verses apply to the major sinner. It provides two key clarifications: sin encompassing from all sides vs. some sides, and interpreting the verse about killing a believer as referring to an unbeliever.
The response uses the distinction between 'ihata' (encompassing) from all sides vs. some sides.
3
Understand the key distinction
The core of the Ash'ari position is that the verses describe a state where sin completely dominates a person, which is true only of the unbeliever. The believer with faith and good deeds is not completely dominated by sin.
This preserves the possibility that believers who commit major sins are not eternally punished.
4
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider how this response supports the Ash'ari doctrine that major sinners who die with faith will eventually be saved, even if they enter Hell temporarily.
This connects to the broader debate on the intermediate position and eternal punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستعانوا بعد إقامة ذلك الدليل العقلي من النقل بوجهين. الأول بآيات تشعر بالخلود كقوله تعالى : من كسب سيئة وأحاطت به خطيئة فأولئك أصحاب النار هم فيها خالدون ﴾ [البقرة : ۸۱]، وقوله : ومن يعص الله ورسوله ويتعد حدوده يدخله ناراً خالداً فيها ﴾ [النساء : ١٤ ] ، وقوله : ومن يقتل مؤمناً متعمداً فجزاؤه خالداً فيها ﴾ [النساء : ۹۳]، قالوا: والخلود حقيقة في الدوام لقوله تعالى: وما جعلنا لبشر من قبلك الخلد ﴾ [الأنبياء : ٣٤] ، مع أنه تعالى قد جعل لكثير المكث الطويل)، فلو حمل الخلود على المكث الطويل لم تصدق هذه الآيات . ( والجواب لا نسلم أن من له حسنات من الإيمان والطاعات فقد أحاطت به خطيئته من بعض جوانبه لا محيطة به (و) لا نسلم ( أن من اكتسب كبيرة فقد تعدى حدوده بل تعدى بعض حدوده والمراد بالآية الثالثة من ( قتل مؤمناً لأنه مؤمن ولا يكون ذلك القاتل إلا كافراً، فالآيات المذكورة لا تتناول صاحب الكبيرة
And they sought support, after establishing that rational proof, from transmitted evidence in two ways. The first: with verses that suggest eternity, such as His statement: 'Whoever earns a sin and his sin encompasses him, those are the companions of the Fire, they will abide therein forever' [2:81], and His statement: 'And whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger and transgresses His limits, He will admit him to a Fire, abiding forever therein' [4:14], and His statement: 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is that he will abide therein forever' [4:93]. They said: 'Eternity (al-khulud) is truly perpetual duration, because of His statement: 'And We did not grant any human before you eternity' [21:34], while He has granted many long stay. So if khulud were interpreted as long stay, these verses would not be true.' (And the answer: We do not concede that one who has good deeds of faith and obedience has his sin encompassing him from all sides—rather from some sides, not completely. And we do not concede that whoever commits a major sin has transgressed His limits; rather he has transgressed some of His limits. And what is meant by the third verse is one who kills a believer because he is a believer, and such a killer is only an unbeliever. So the mentioned verses do not include the major sinner.)
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage presents the first transmitted argument used by the Mu'tazila to prove that major sinners will be eternally punished, and the Ash'ari response. The passage is an objection-and-answer structure. The Mu'tazila cite three verses that appear to promise eternal punishment for sinners, and they argue that the word 'khulud' must mean eternal duration because God says He did not grant 'khuld' to any human before, whereas long stay is granted to many. The Ash'ari response is twofold: first, they deny that these verses apply to the major sinner (sahib al-kabira). The verses about sin encompassing and transgression of limits refer to someone whose sins completely dominate him—that is the unbeliever. The third verse about killing a believer is interpreted as referring to a killer who is a non-believer, because only an unbeliever would kill a believer for his faith. Thus the verses do not include the major sinner who has faith and other good deeds. This preserves the possibility of eventual salvation for sinful believers.

Key Terms
خلود
صاحب الكبيرة
كبيرة
إيمان
وعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic verses on eternal punishment apply to all sinners, including believers who commit major sins.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that these verses describe a state where sin completely dominates a person (ihata), which is only true of unbelievers. Believers with faith are not completely dominated by their sins, so the verses do not apply to them.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response, why does the verse 'Whoever earns a sin and his sin encompasses him' not apply to the major sinner who has faith?
A) Because the sin does not completely dominate him as he also has good deeds of faith.
B) Because the verse is abrogated.
C) Because the word 'sin' in the verse refers only to unbelief.
D) Because major sins are forgiven automatically.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari refutation of the Mu'tazila's first transmitted argument hinges on the condition that the sin must 'encompass' the person entirely, which only applies to unbelievers, not to believers who commit major sins.

How does the Ash'ari condition that sin must completely encompass a person challenge you to examine the state of your own heart? What specific step can you take today to break free from a sin that is threatening to encompass you?
?
The Mu'tazila were concerned that a lenient view of punishment would lead to laxity in avoiding sin. The Linguistic Meaning of Khulud: Long Stay or Eternal?
Linguistic argument that khulud means long stay.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari argument uses linguistic evidence to show that khulud (خلود) literally means 'long stay' (مكث طويل), which can include eternity but is not synonymous with it.

Core Idea
Khulud literally means long stay, not necessarily eternal.

In the debate about the eternal punishment of grave sinners, the Mu'tazila argued that khulud in the Qur'an means eternal duration. The Ash'ari response is linguistic: the primary meaning of khulud is simply a long stay, which can be temporary or perpetual. This lesson examines the evidence from common Arabic usage and how it resolves the theological dispute without resorting to metaphor or homonymy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recall that the debate is about whether sinful believers will be in Hell forever. The Mu'tazila claim khulud means eternal, so the verses threaten eternity. The Ash'ari argue khulud primarily means long stay.
This is part of the larger discussion on al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn.
2
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly. Notice the key terms: خلود, دوام, مكث طويل, مجاز, اشتراك.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
3
Identify the linguistic evidence
The passage cites common expressions: مخلد, وقف مخلد, خلد الله ملكه. These clearly mean long duration, not necessarily eternity. This shows that the literal meaning is long stay.
Think about how these words are used in everyday language.
4
Understand the interpretive principle
To avoid مجاز or اشتراك, it is better to take the literal meaning as long stay, and let context specify if eternity is meant. The verse about no human having khuld is interpreted by قرينة الحال as referring to the perpetual kind of long stay.
This principle of interpretation is crucial in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا تناولها إياه لكن الخلود المذكور فيها هو المكث الطويل وما ذكرتم ) من الاستدلال على أنه حقيقة في الدوام ( معارض بما يقال ) في الاستعمال الشائع : مخلد ووقف مخلد وخلد الله ملكه والمراد طول المدة بلا شبهة فالأولى حينئذ أن يجعل حقيقة في المكث الطويل سواء كان معه دوام أولا ، احتراز عن لزوم المجاز أو الاشتراك ) والآية المذكورة حملناها على الدوام الذي هو أحد قسمي المكث الطويل ( لقرينة الحال)، فلا يلزم مجاز لأن خصوصية ذلك القسم مستفادة من خارج لا مقصودة بنفس اللفظ
We grant that they include him, but the khulud mentioned in them is the long stay. And what you mentioned—from the evidence that it is truly perpetual duration—is countered by what is said in common usage: 'immortalized (mukhallad),' 'an endowment made perpetual (waqf mukhallad),' and 'may God perpetuate his kingdom (khallada Allah mulkahu),' and what is meant is long duration without doubt. So it is better then to take it as literally meaning long stay, whether or not it is accompanied by perpetuity, as a safeguard against the necessity of metaphor (majaz) or polysemy (ishtirak). And the mentioned verse [about no human granted khuld] we have interpreted it as referring to perpetuity which is one of the two kinds of long stay, due to the contextual indication (qarinat al-hal), so no metaphor is required because the specificity of that kind is derived from outside, not intended by the word itself.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is a further response in the argument about the meaning of khulud. Even if we grant that the verses cover major sinners, the term khulud does not necessarily mean eternal duration; it can mean a long stay. The Mu'tazila argued that khulud literally means perpetuity (dawam) citing the verse that God granted no human khuld before. The Ash'ari responds by showing that in common usage, words like 'mukhallad' (immortalized), 'waqf mukhallad' (perpetual endowment), and 'khallada Allah mulkahu' (may God perpetuate his kingdom) are used to indicate long duration, not necessarily eternity. To avoid metaphor (majaz) or homonymy (ishtirak), it is better to take khulud as literally meaning long stay, which can include both temporary long stay and perpetual stay. The verse about no human before having khuld is interpreted as referring to the specific kind of long stay that is perpetual, but that is due to contextual evidence, not the word itself. Thus when the Qur'an speaks of khulud for sinners, it can mean a very long stay, not necessarily eternal.

Key Terms
خلود (khulud)
دوام (dawam)
مكث طويل (mukth tawil)
مجاز (majaz)
اشتراك (ishtirak)
قرينة الحال (qarinat al-hal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Arabic word khulud (خلود) always and literally means eternal duration without end.
Correction: The passage shows that in common usage, khulud and its derivatives (e.g., mukhallad) indicate a long stay, which may or may not be eternal. The literal meaning is long stay, and eternity is a specific kind of long stay that can be indicated by context.

Checkpoint
How does the Ash'ari refute the claim that khulud literally means perpetual duration?
A) By citing examples where khulud refers to long stay, not eternity.
B) By denying the verse about no human having khuld.
C) By saying that khulud means only a thousand years.
D) By saying that the word is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
Khulud literally means 'long stay'; its specific reference to eternity depends on context, not the word itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses about khulud in Hell, do you instinctively assume it means never-ending torment, or do you now pause to consider it might mean a very long stay? How does this shift in understanding affect your fear of Allah's punishment and your hope for His mercy? Commit to memorizing one verse about khulud and reflecting on its context.
?
This lesson shows that taking a word literally (as long stay) can open up a broader understanding of Allah's justice and mercy. Are there any Islamic concepts you have unconsciously defined in a rigid or extreme way because you assumed certain words always carry their strongest meaning? Write down one such concept and resolve to study its linguistic range.

The Ash'ari scholar avoided metaphor to protect the literal meaning of the Qur'an. Do you ever force explanations onto difficult verses to make them more comfortable for yourself? Challenge yourself to accept the literal meaning within its proper context, even if it challenges your previous understanding, and ask Allah to guide you to the truth.
