The Second Objection from Surah al-Infitar and Its Ash'ari Answer
Presents the second transmitted objection and the Ash'ari response on the interpretation of the verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari interpret the verse about the wicked in hell to avoid implying eternal punishment for the sinful believer.

Core Idea
The verse about the wicked is about deserving punishment, not actual presence.

The Mu'tazila cite Surah al-Infitar to argue that the wicked will be eternally punished. The Ash'ari respond by examining the term 'fujjar' and the apparent meaning of the verse. This lesson explores that initial response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the response
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow: the Mu'tazila claim, then the Ash'ari reply.
Notice the verse citation and its apparent implication.
2
Understand the two parts of the Ash'ari answer
The answer has two moves: first, defining 'fujjar' as complete wickedness (unbelief), and second, interpreting the verse as referring to deserving punishment, not actual presence.
The second point resolves the apparent contradiction that the wicked are not in hell now.
3
See how this protects the sinful believer
By limiting 'fujjar' to unbelievers and interpreting the verse as 'deserving', the Ash'ari avoid the conclusion that believing sinners are eternally punished.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني) من الوجهين (قوله تعالى : ( وإن الفجار لفي جحيم يصلونها يوم الدين وما هم عنها بغائبين ﴾ [ الانفطار : ٤١-١٥] ولو خرجوا عنها لكانوا غائبين عنها . الجواب : ( عن هذه الآية وحدها أن لفظ الفجار لا يتناول إلا من هو كامل في فجوره وهو الكافر كما يدل عليه قوله : أولئك هم الكفرة الفجرة ﴾ [عبس: ٤٢] ، وأيضاً ظاهرها يقتضي كون الفجار في الجحيم في الحال ومعلوم أنه ليس الأمر كذلك فوجب التأويل باستحقاق النار وعدم غيبتهم عن استحقاقها لكن الله أخرهم عنها برحمته مع اتصافهم بذلك الاستحقاق . والجواب عنها وعما قبلها من الآيات المذكورة في الوجه الأول المعارضة بالآيات الدالة على الوعد بالثواب نحو قوله تعالى : ( فمن يعمل مثقال ذرة خيراً يره ﴾ [الزلزلة : ٧] و ) ، قوله : ( ويجزي الذين أحسنوا بالحسنى ﴾ [ النجم : ۳۱] و)، قوله : (هل جزاء الإحسان إلا الإحسان ﴾ [الرحمن : ٦٠] فقد ثبت لصاحب الكبيرة بإيمانه وسائر ما يكون له من الحسنات استحقاق الثواب ( وهو عندهم ينافي استحقاق العقاب فضلاً عن كونه مخلداً في العقوبة فلا تكون تلك الآية عامة متناولة له ( وإن سلمنا) عمومها إياه فيجب تخصيصها بالآيات الدالة على اختصاص العذاب بالكفار نحو قوله تعالى : إنا قد أوحي إلينا أن العذاب على من كذب وتولى [ طه : ٤٨ ] ، وقوله : إن الخزي اليوم والسوء على الكافرين ﴾ [النحل : ٢٧ ] ، وقوله : وكلما ألقي فيها فوج ﴾ [الملك : ۸] ، إلى قوله : فكذبنا وقلنا ما نزل الله شيء [ الملك : ۹ ] واعلم أن اختصاص العذاب ( مطلقاً ( بالكفار مذهب مقاتل بن سليمان من المفسرين (و) مذهب ( المرجئة عملاً بظاهر هذه الآيات لكنا يخصصها بالعذاب المؤيد جمعاً بينها وبين الأدلة الدالة على وعيد الفساق ) .
The second of the two ways: His statement: 'Indeed the wicked are in hellfire, they will roast therein on the Day of Judgment, and they will not be absent from it' [82:14-15]. If they were to leave it, they would be absent from it. The answer regarding this verse alone: The word 'fujjar' (wicked) only includes one who is complete in his wickedness, and that is the unbeliever, as indicated by His statement: 'Those are the unbelievers, the wicked' [80:42]. Also, its apparent meaning implies that the wicked are in hellfire at the present moment, and it is known that this is not the case, so it must be interpreted as deserving the fire and not being absent from deserving it, but God has delayed it from them by His mercy while they are characterized by that deserving. And the answer regarding it and the verses mentioned in the first way: They are countered by the verses indicating the promise of reward, such as His statement: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' [99:7], and His statement: 'And He will reward those who do good with the best [reward]' [53:31], and His statement: 'Is the reward of goodness except goodness?' [55:60]. So it is established for the major sinner, on account of his faith and the rest of his good deeds, a right to reward. And according to them, this right conflicts with deserving punishment, let alone being eternally punished. So that verse is not general and does not include him. And if we grant its generality including him, then it must be specified by the verses indicating that punishment is exclusively for the unbelievers, such as His statement: 'Indeed it has been revealed to us that the punishment is upon whoever denies and turns away' [20:48], and His statement: 'Indeed disgrace today and evil are upon the disbelievers' [16:27], and His statement: 'Whenever a group is thrown into it' [67:8] until His statement: 'So we denied and said, "Allah has not sent down anything"' [67:9]. And know that the exclusivity of punishment absolutely to unbelievers is the doctrine of Muqatil ibn Sulayman among the exegetes, and the doctrine of the Murji'ah in acting upon the apparent meaning of these verses. But we specify them with perpetual punishment, reconciling between them and the evidence indicating the threat against the wicked.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage presents the second transmitted argument from the Mu'tazila and a two-part Ash'ari response. The Mu'tazila cite Surah al-Infitar verses that state the wicked will be in hell on the Day of Judgment and will not be absent from it, implying eternal presence. The Ash'ari response first addresses this verse alone: the word 'fujjar' (wicked) in its complete sense refers only to the unbeliever, as shown by another verse. Moreover, the literal sense implies they are already in hell, which is not the case, so the verse must be interpreted as meaning they deserve the fire and will not be absent from that deserving, though God may delay their actual punishment. The second part of the response confronts all the proof texts with verses that promise reward for good deeds. Since the major sinner has faith and good deeds, he has a right to reward that, according to the Mu'tazila, conflicts with punishment. Even if we accept the verses' generality, they must be specified by verses that limit punishment to unbelievers. The passage notes that Muqatil ibn Sulayman and the Murji'ah hold that punishment is exclusively for unbelievers, but the Ash'ari position specifies it as perpetual punishment to reconcile the conflicting evidence.

Key Terms
فجار (fujjar)
استحقاق (istihqaq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'the wicked are in hellfire... and they will not be absent from it' means that all wicked people (including sinful believers) will actually be in hell forever.
Correction: The Ash'ari argue that the term 'fujjar' refers only to complete wickedness (unbelievers), and the verse is about deserving punishment, not actual presence. So it does not apply to believing sinners.

Checkpoint
How does the Ash'ari interpret the verse 'the wicked are in hellfire... and they will not be absent from it'?
A) It means they physically remain in hell forever.
B) It means they deserve the fire and will not be absent from that deserving.
C) It means they will be in hell for a limited time.
D) It means the verse is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari argue that the verse about the wicked uses the term to refer to unbelievers and interprets their presence in hell as a state of deserving, not actual presence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever feel that you are already counted among 'the wicked' and doomed to hell? How does this lesson's teaching that the verse refers only to unbelievers and that you may still deserve but be spared by God's mercy change the way you view yourself after sinning? Will you this week consciously remind yourself of God's mercy rather than despair?

This lesson demonstrates that understanding the Quran requires careful attention to context and other verses. Is there an area of your life where you have been quick to judge others or yourself based on a single verse without deeper study? What specific step will you take this week to learn the context of a verse you have misunderstood?
?
The Ash'ari response preserves the possibility of mercy for sinful believers. Do you truly hope for God's mercy for yourself and for fellow Muslims who commit major sins, or do you secretly wish for their punishment? How can you cultivate genuine hope for others' salvation in your heart starting today?

The Combined Response: Verses of Promise and Specification
Presents the Ash'ari response using verses of reward, specification, and mention of Muqatil and Murji'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari use verses of reward and specification to counter the Mu'tazila proof texts and distinguish their position from the Murji'ah.

Core Idea
Verses of promise protect the believer's reward, and verses of threat are specified to unbelievers.

The Ash'ari answer the Mu'tazila proof texts by countering with verses that promise reward for good deeds and verses that specify punishment for unbelievers. They also note the positions of Muqatil and the Murji'ah. This lesson unpacks that combined argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the combined response
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'And the answer regarding it and the verses mentioned in the first way...'
This is a broader response addressing all the proof texts together.
2
Identify the three verses of reward
Find the three Qur'anic verses that promise reward for good deeds. Understand how they establish the believer's right to reward.
These verses are from Surah az-Zalzalah, an-Najm, and ar-Rahman.
3
Understand the specification argument
Even if the threat verses are general, they must be specified by verses that limit punishment to unbelievers. The Ash'ari list several such verses.
4
Compare with Muqatil and Murji'ah positions
The Ash'ari note that Muqatil and the Murji'ah take the verses at face value to mean no punishment for believers. But the Ash'ari specify perpetual punishment for unbelievers only, reconciling all evidence.
The Ash'ari do not deny punishment for sinful believers; they affirm temporary punishment.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني) من الوجهين (قوله تعالى : ( وإن الفجار لفي جحيم يصلونها يوم الدين وما هم عنها بغائبين ﴾ [ الانفطار : ٤١-١٥] ولو خرجوا عنها لكانوا غائبين عنها . الجواب : ( عن هذه الآية وحدها أن لفظ الفجار لا يتناول إلا من هو كامل في فجوره وهو الكافر كما يدل عليه قوله : أولئك هم الكفرة الفجرة ﴾ [عبس: ٤٢] ، وأيضاً ظاهرها يقتضي كون الفجار في الجحيم في الحال ومعلوم أنه ليس الأمر كذلك فوجب التأويل باستحقاق النار وعدم غيبتهم عن استحقاقها لكن الله أخرهم عنها برحمته مع اتصافهم بذلك الاستحقاق . والجواب عنها وعما قبلها من الآيات المذكورة في الوجه الأول المعارضة بالآيات الدالة على الوعد بالثواب نحو قوله تعالى : ( فمن يعمل مثقال ذرة خيراً يره ﴾ [الزلزلة : ٧] و ) ، قوله : ( ويجزي الذين أحسنوا بالحسنى ﴾ [ النجم : ۳۱] و)، قوله : (هل جزاء الإحسان إلا الإحسان ﴾ [الرحمن : ٦٠] فقد ثبت لصاحب الكبيرة بإيمانه وسائر ما يكون له من الحسنات استحقاق الثواب ( وهو عندهم ينافي استحقاق العقاب فضلاً عن كونه مخلداً في العقوبة فلا تكون تلك الآية عامة متناولة له ( وإن سلمنا) عمومها إياه فيجب تخصيصها بالآيات الدالة على اختصاص العذاب بالكفار نحو قوله تعالى : إنا قد أوحي إلينا أن العذاب على من كذب وتولى [ طه : ٤٨ ] ، وقوله : إن الخزي اليوم والسوء على الكافرين ﴾ [النحل : ٢٧ ] ، وقوله : وكلما ألقي فيها فوج ﴾ [الملك : ۸] ، إلى قوله : فكذبنا وقلنا ما نزل الله شيء [ الملك : ۹ ] واعلم أن اختصاص العذاب ( مطلقاً ( بالكفار مذهب مقاتل بن سليمان من المفسرين (و) مذهب ( المرجئة عملاً بظاهر هذه الآيات لكنا يخصصها بالعذاب المؤيد جمعاً بينها وبين الأدلة الدالة على وعيد الفساق ) .
The second of the two ways: His statement: 'Indeed the wicked are in hellfire, they will roast therein on the Day of Judgment, and they will not be absent from it' [82:14-15]. If they were to leave it, they would be absent from it. The answer regarding this verse alone: The word 'fujjar' (wicked) only includes one who is complete in his wickedness, and that is the unbeliever, as indicated by His statement: 'Those are the unbelievers, the wicked' [80:42]. Also, its apparent meaning implies that the wicked are in hellfire at the present moment, and it is known that this is not the case, so it must be interpreted as deserving the fire and not being absent from deserving it, but God has delayed it from them by His mercy while they are characterized by that deserving. And the answer regarding it and the verses mentioned in the first way: They are countered by the verses indicating the promise of reward, such as His statement: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' [99:7], and His statement: 'And He will reward those who do good with the best [reward]' [53:31], and His statement: 'Is the reward of goodness except goodness?' [55:60]. So it is established for the major sinner, on account of his faith and the rest of his good deeds, a right to reward. And according to them, this right conflicts with deserving punishment, let alone being eternally punished. So that verse is not general and does not include him. And if we grant its generality including him, then it must be specified by the verses indicating that punishment is exclusively for the unbelievers, such as His statement: 'Indeed it has been revealed to us that the punishment is upon whoever denies and turns away' [20:48], and His statement: 'Indeed disgrace today and evil are upon the disbelievers' [16:27], and His statement: 'Whenever a group is thrown into it' [67:8] until His statement: 'So we denied and said, "Allah has not sent down anything"' [67:9]. And know that the exclusivity of punishment absolutely to unbelievers is the doctrine of Muqatil ibn Sulayman among the exegetes, and the doctrine of the Murji'ah in acting upon the apparent meaning of these verses. But we specify them with perpetual punishment, reconciling between them and the evidence indicating the threat against the wicked.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage presents the second transmitted argument from the Mu'tazila and a two-part Ash'ari response. The Mu'tazila cite Surah al-Infitar verses that state the wicked will be in hell on the Day of Judgment and will not be absent from it, implying eternal presence. The Ash'ari response first addresses this verse alone: the word 'fujjar' (wicked) in its complete sense refers only to the unbeliever, as shown by another verse. Moreover, the literal sense implies they are already in hell, which is not the case, so the verse must be interpreted as meaning they deserve the fire and will not be absent from that deserving, though God may delay their actual punishment. The second part of the response confronts all the proof texts with verses that promise reward for good deeds. Since the major sinner has faith and good deeds, he has a right to reward that, according to the Mu'tazila, conflicts with punishment. Even if we accept the verses' generality, they must be specified by verses that limit punishment to unbelievers. The passage notes that Muqatil ibn Sulayman and the Murji'ah hold that punishment is exclusively for unbelievers, but the Ash'ari position specifies it as perpetual punishment to reconcile the conflicting evidence.

Key Terms
المرجئة (al-Murji'ah)
مقاتل بن سليمان (Muqatil ibn Sulayman)
الإحباط (al-ihbat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari position is identical to the Murji'ah: that believers will never be punished for sins.
Correction: The Ash'ari explicitly differ: they specify the threat verses as perpetual punishment for unbelievers only, but accept that sinful believers may be punished temporarily (not eternally).

Checkpoint
How do the Ash'ari reconcile the threat verses with the promise verses?
A) They argue threat verses are abrogated.
B) They interpret threat verses as referring to unbelievers only and accept temporary punishment for sinful believers.
C) They follow the Murji'ah in denying any punishment for believers.
D) They claim the promise verses are not general.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari reconcile conflicting verses by specifying the threat of eternal punishment to unbelievers while affirming that sinful believers may be punished temporarily but not eternally, unlike the Murji'ah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your good deeds, however small, guarantee you a reward from Allah, or do you live in fear that your sins outweigh them? How does this lesson change your daily calculation of hope and fear?
?
When you sin, do you secretly despair of Allah's mercy, assuming you are beyond hope? Or do you presume upon His mercy, ignoring the threat of punishment? This lesson forces a balance: what is one concrete change you will make tonight to embody both hope and fear?
?
The Murji'ah denied any punishment for believers. Is there any area in your life where you comfort yourself with the idea that 'Allah will forgive anyway,' without taking His warnings seriously? Will you now let the threat verses pierce your heart while holding fast to the promise verses?

Reward as Grace, Punishment as Justice
Defines reward as grace and punishment as justice, with promise/threat distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definitions of reward as a grace from God and punishment as an act of justice, and why breaking a threat is not considered a defect.

Core Idea
Reward is grace, punishment is justice, and God's threats are not binding promises.

The Ash'ari school defines reward and punishment in a way that balances God's mercy and justice. This passage lays down the foundational definitions, distinguishing between a promise (which God must keep to avoid defect) and a threat (which He may break as an act of grace).

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of reward and punishment
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how reward is described as 'grace' and punishment as 'justice'.
The text uses the terms فضل (grace) and عدل (justice).
2
Identify the promise-threat distinction
Notice the different treatment of breaking a promise vs. breaking a threat. The text says breaking a promise is a defect, but breaking a threat is not; in fact, God is praised for it.
This distinction is central to Ash'ari theology.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: How does this definition uphold both God's justice and His mercy? Why is forgiveness considered grace?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السادس : في تقرير مذهب الثواب والعقاب ] من ( في تقرير مذهب أصحابنا : في الثواب والعقاب وما يتعلق بهما وفيه مباحث : لأن الأول : قالوا : الثواب فضل من الله تعالى وعد به فيفي به من غير وجوب الخلف في الوعد نقص تعالى الله عنه ( وأما عدم الوجوب فلما مر مرارا (و) قالوا ( العقاب عدل ) من الله لأن الكل ملكه ( فله أن يتصرف فيه ( كما يشاء وله العفو عنه لأنه فضل ولا يعد الخلف في الوعيد نقصاً ) بل يمدح به (عند العقلاء) المبحث قوله : ( وهو عندهم ينافي استحقاق العقاب ) يدفعون هذا بقضية الإحباط كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( لأن الخلف في الوعد نقص فيه بحث لأنه إن أراد أنه نقص في الصفة فممنوع وإن أراد أنه نقص في الفعل فهو القبح العقلي بعينه كما اعترف به المصنف في امتناع الكذب عليه تعالى والأشاعرة لا يقولون به فكيف الاستدلال به على وفاء الوعد على أنه إذا كان نقصاً يجب تنزيه الله تعالى عنه فيفيد وجوب الثواب وهو خلاف المذهب، فالأولى هاهنا التمسك بأن الخلف كذب، والكذب منتف في كلامه انتفاء معلوماً بالضرورة من الدين كما سبق وإن أمكن عقلاً. قوله : ( ولا يعد الخلف في الوعيد نقصاً على مادل عليه قول الشاعر: وإني وإن أوعدته أو وعدتـــــه لمخلف إيعادي ومنجز موعدي ويرد عليه أن فيه كذباً وقد دل الإجماع على انتفائه وتبديلاً للقول وقد قال الله تعالى: ما يبدل القول لدي ﴾ [ ق : ۲۹]، وما قيل : إن الكذب إنما يكون في الماضي دون المستقبل فلا يخفى فساده فالوجه أن يقال : الوعيد مصروف إلى الإخبار عن استحقاق العقاب لا عن وقوعه حتى يلزم الخلف بالعفو، وقد يقال : الوعد ليس بإخبار عن وقوع الموعود في المستقبل، بل إنشاء عزم على إيقاعه وكذا الإيعاد فلا كذب في الإخلاف في شيء منهما، وقيل: لعل المراد بالقول في قوله تعالى ما يبدل القول لدي القول البات كقوله تعالى: لأملان جهنم [الأعراف : ۱۸ ] ، وأما عمومات الوعيد مع التنصيص على العفو في الجملة، فليس من ذلك .
The Sixth Objective: On establishing the doctrine of reward and punishment. From (On establishing the doctrine of our companions: concerning reward and punishment and what pertains to them, and it contains investigations. Because the First: They said: Reward is a grace from God Almighty, which He promised and He fulfills it without obligation. Breaking a promise is a defect—exalted is God above it. (As for the lack of obligation, it is due to what has been repeatedly mentioned. And) They said: Punishment is justice from God because everything is His property, so He may dispose of it as He wills, and He may forgive it because it is grace. And breaking a threat is not considered a defect; rather, He is praised for it among rational people. (Section). His saying: (And it is, according to them, incompatible with deserving punishment) they refute this by the principle of nullification (al-ihbāt), as is not hidden. His saying: (Because breaking a promise is a defect) there is discussion in it. If he means it is a defect in the attribute, it is denied; if he means it is a defect in the act, then it is precisely rational evil (al-qubḥ al-ʿaqlī), as the author acknowledged in the impossibility of lying upon Him—exalted is He—and the Ashʿarites do not affirm it. So how can it be used as evidence for fulfilling the promise? Moreover, if it were a defect, it would be obligatory to declare God—exalted is He—free from it, which would entail the obligation of reward, and that is contrary to the school. So the better approach here is to adhere to the fact that breaking a promise is a lie, and lying is negated from His speech by a negation known necessarily from religion, even if it is rationally possible. His saying: (And breaking a threat is not considered a defect) as indicated by the poet's saying: 'And indeed, whether I threaten him or promise him, I am one who breaks his threat and fulfills his promise.' It is objected that in it there is lying, and consensus has indicated its negation, and it is a substitution of speech, and God—exalted is He—said: 'My word is not changed' (Q 50:29). And what was said: that lying only occurs in the past, not the future—its unsoundness is not hidden. So the correct view is to say: The threat is directed to informing about deserving punishment, not about its occurrence, so that breaking it would necessitate a pardon. And it may be said: The promise is not an announcement of the occurrence of the promised thing in the future, but rather an establishment of resolve to bring it about. Similarly with the threat. Thus there is no lie in breaking any of them. And it was said: Perhaps what is meant by 'word' in His saying 'My word is not changed' is the definitive word, like His saying: 'I will surely fill Hell' (Q 7:18). As for the general threats with the specification of pardon in general, that is not from that.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage defines the Ashʿarī position on reward and punishment, then explains and defends the key distinction between promise and threat. The definition states that reward (al-thawāb) is a grace (faḍl) from God—He promised it and will fulfill it, not because it is obligatory, but because breaking a promise would be a defect (naqṣ), and God is exalted above defects. Punishment (al-ʿiqāb) is justice (ʿadl) because everything belongs to God, so He may punish or forgive as He wills; forgiveness is grace. Crucially, breaking a threat (al-waʿīd) is not a defect; in fact, God is praised for it. The commentary then addresses an objection: if reward and punishment are deserved, why does the threat not apply? The answer invokes the principle of nullification (al-ihbāt—where good deeds nullify punishment for major sins). Then it examines the claim that breaking a promise is a defect. The commentator argues that if 'defect' means a defect in God's attribute, it is denied; if it means a defect in the act, that implies rational evil (al-qubḥ al-ʿaqlī), which Ashʿarites reject. So the better argument is that breaking a promise is a lie, and lying is necessarily excluded from God's speech by religion, even if rationally possible. Regarding the threat, the commentator rebuts the poet's analogy by noting that breaking a threat still involves either lying (if it is an informative statement) or a change of speech. Several solutions are offered: (1) the threat is about deserving punishment, not its occurrence; (2) promises and threats are not informational statements but expressions of resolve, so breaking them is not a lie; (3) the definitive word (al-qawl al-bāt) about filling Hell is absolute, while general threats are qualified by the possibility of pardon. The passage concludes that general threats with specified pardon are not absolute.

Key Terms
الثواب
العقاب
الفضل
العدل
الوعد
الوعيد
الخلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris believe God can punish arbitrarily without reason.
Correction: Punishment is just because God owns everything; forgiveness is an act of grace.

Checkpoint
What does the Ash'ari school say about breaking a promise and breaking a threat?
A) Both are defects.
B) Breaking a promise is a defect, but breaking a threat is not.
C) Neither is a defect.
D) Breaking a promise is not a defect, but breaking a threat is.

Key Takeaway
Reward is God's grace, punishment is His justice, and breaking a threat is not a defect but a praiseworthy act of mercy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you secretly believe that your good deeds obligate God to reward you? How does the lesson's teaching that reward is pure grace change your sense of entitlement in worship?
?
When you sin, do you fear God's punishment as a necessary outcome, or do you hope for His forgiveness? How does knowing that God is praised for pardoning despite His threat affect your repentance?
?
If God's threats are not binding, does that make you take them less seriously? Reflect on whether your fear of God is rooted in His justice or in His mercy, and what that reveals about your heart.

Defending the Promise vs. Threat Distinction
Defends the promise-threat distinction through detailed argumentation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari theologians defend the distinction between breaking a promise and breaking a threat through arguments about defect, lying, and interpretation of threats.

Core Idea
Breaking a promise is a lie, but breaking a threat can be an act of grace.

After defining reward and punishment, the text addresses objections. The main challenge is why breaking a promise is a defect but breaking a threat is not. The commentator analyzes the meanings of defect, invokes the principle of nullification, and offers several solutions regarding the nature of threats.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and nullification response
The passage begins with an objection that reward and punishment are incompatible with deserving punishment. The response uses the principle of nullification (al-ihbāt).
Nullification means good deeds cancel punishment for major sins.
2
Analyze the 'defect' argument
The text examines the claim that breaking a promise is a defect. It distinguishes between defect in attribute and defect in act, and rejects both from an Ash'ari perspective.
The author suggests the better argument is that breaking a promise is a lie.
3
Examine the lie argument
Breaking a promise is a lie, and lying is necessarily negated from God by religion, even if rationally possible.
This argument does not rely on rational evil (qubḥ 'aqlī).
4
Consider the solutions for threats
Several solutions are proposed: threats inform about deserving punishment, not occurrence; promises/threats are expressions of resolve, not information; and definitive threats are absolute while general ones allow pardon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السادس : في تقرير مذهب الثواب والعقاب ] من ( في تقرير مذهب أصحابنا : في الثواب والعقاب وما يتعلق بهما وفيه مباحث : لأن الأول : قالوا : الثواب فضل من الله تعالى وعد به فيفي به من غير وجوب الخلف في الوعد نقص تعالى الله عنه ( وأما عدم الوجوب فلما مر مرارا (و) قالوا ( العقاب عدل ) من الله لأن الكل ملكه ( فله أن يتصرف فيه ( كما يشاء وله العفو عنه لأنه فضل ولا يعد الخلف في الوعيد نقصاً ) بل يمدح به (عند العقلاء) المبحث قوله : ( وهو عندهم ينافي استحقاق العقاب ) يدفعون هذا بقضية الإحباط كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( لأن الخلف في الوعد نقص فيه بحث لأنه إن أراد أنه نقص في الصفة فممنوع وإن أراد أنه نقص في الفعل فهو القبح العقلي بعينه كما اعترف به المصنف في امتناع الكذب عليه تعالى والأشاعرة لا يقولون به فكيف الاستدلال به على وفاء الوعد على أنه إذا كان نقصاً يجب تنزيه الله تعالى عنه فيفيد وجوب الثواب وهو خلاف المذهب، فالأولى هاهنا التمسك بأن الخلف كذب، والكذب منتف في كلامه انتفاء معلوماً بالضرورة من الدين كما سبق وإن أمكن عقلاً. قوله : ( ولا يعد الخلف في الوعيد نقصاً على مادل عليه قول الشاعر: وإني وإن أوعدته أو وعدتـــــه لمخلف إيعادي ومنجز موعدي ويرد عليه أن فيه كذباً وقد دل الإجماع على انتفائه وتبديلاً للقول وقد قال الله تعالى: ما يبدل القول لدي ﴾ [ ق : ۲۹]، وما قيل : إن الكذب إنما يكون في الماضي دون المستقبل فلا يخفى فساده فالوجه أن يقال : الوعيد مصروف إلى الإخبار عن استحقاق العقاب لا عن وقوعه حتى يلزم الخلف بالعفو، وقد يقال : الوعد ليس بإخبار عن وقوع الموعود في المستقبل، بل إنشاء عزم على إيقاعه وكذا الإيعاد فلا كذب في الإخلاف في شيء منهما، وقيل: لعل المراد بالقول في قوله تعالى ما يبدل القول لدي القول البات كقوله تعالى: لأملان جهنم [الأعراف : ۱۸ ] ، وأما عمومات الوعيد مع التنصيص على العفو في الجملة، فليس من ذلك .
The Sixth Objective: On establishing the doctrine of reward and punishment. From (On establishing the doctrine of our companions: concerning reward and punishment and what pertains to them, and it contains investigations. Because the First: They said: Reward is a grace from God Almighty, which He promised and He fulfills it without obligation. Breaking a promise is a defect—exalted is God above it. (As for the lack of obligation, it is due to what has been repeatedly mentioned. And) They said: Punishment is justice from God because everything is His property, so He may dispose of it as He wills, and He may forgive it because it is grace. And breaking a threat is not considered a defect; rather, He is praised for it among rational people. (Section). His saying: (And it is, according to them, incompatible with deserving punishment) they refute this by the principle of nullification (al-ihbāt), as is not hidden. His saying: (Because breaking a promise is a defect) there is discussion in it. If he means it is a defect in the attribute, it is denied; if he means it is a defect in the act, then it is precisely rational evil (al-qubḥ al-ʿaqlī), as the author acknowledged in the impossibility of lying upon Him—exalted is He—and the Ashʿarites do not affirm it. So how can it be used as evidence for fulfilling the promise? Moreover, if it were a defect, it would be obligatory to declare God—exalted is He—free from it, which would entail the obligation of reward, and that is contrary to the school. So the better approach here is to adhere to the fact that breaking a promise is a lie, and lying is negated from His speech by a negation known necessarily from religion, even if it is rationally possible. His saying: (And breaking a threat is not considered a defect) as indicated by the poet's saying: 'And indeed, whether I threaten him or promise him, I am one who breaks his threat and fulfills his promise.' It is objected that in it there is lying, and consensus has indicated its negation, and it is a substitution of speech, and God—exalted is He—said: 'My word is not changed' (Q 50:29). And what was said: that lying only occurs in the past, not the future—its unsoundness is not hidden. So the correct view is to say: The threat is directed to informing about deserving punishment, not about its occurrence, so that breaking it would necessitate a pardon. And it may be said: The promise is not an announcement of the occurrence of the promised thing in the future, but rather an establishment of resolve to bring it about. Similarly with the threat. Thus there is no lie in breaking any of them. And it was said: Perhaps what is meant by 'word' in His saying 'My word is not changed' is the definitive word, like His saying: 'I will surely fill Hell' (Q 7:18). As for the general threats with the specification of pardon in general, that is not from that.

Commentary
This passage defines the Ashʿarī position on reward and punishment, then explains and defends the key distinction between promise and threat. The definition states that reward (al-thawāb) is a grace (faḍl) from God—He promised it and will fulfill it, not because it is obligatory, but because breaking a promise would be a defect (naqṣ), and God is exalted above defects. Punishment (al-ʿiqāb) is justice (ʿadl) because everything belongs to God, so He may punish or forgive as He wills; forgiveness is grace. Crucially, breaking a threat (al-waʿīd) is not a defect; in fact, God is praised for it. The commentary then addresses an objection: if reward and punishment are deserved, why does the threat not apply? The answer invokes the principle of nullification (al-ihbāt—where good deeds nullify punishment for major sins). Then it examines the claim that breaking a promise is a defect. The commentator argues that if 'defect' means a defect in God's attribute, it is denied; if it means a defect in the act, that implies rational evil (al-qubḥ al-ʿaqlī), which Ashʿarites reject. So the better argument is that breaking a promise is a lie, and lying is necessarily excluded from God's speech by religion, even if rationally possible. Regarding the threat, the commentator rebuts the poet's analogy by noting that breaking a threat still involves either lying (if it is an informative statement) or a change of speech. Several solutions are offered: (1) the threat is about deserving punishment, not its occurrence; (2) promises and threats are not informational statements but expressions of resolve, so breaking them is not a lie; (3) the definitive word (al-qawl al-bāt) about filling Hell is absolute, while general threats are qualified by the possibility of pardon. The passage concludes that general threats with specified pardon are not absolute.

Key Terms
الإحباط
الخلف
الكذب
القبح العقلي
الوعد
الوعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God breaking a threat is a form of lying.
Correction: Threats are about deserving punishment, not its occurrence, so no lie occurs if He pardons. Alternatively, threats are expressions of resolve, not informative statements.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is breaking a promise considered a lie but breaking a threat is not?
A) Because a promise is always about the future and a threat about the past.
B) Because breaking a promise is a lie, and lying is impossible for God by religion, while threats can be interpreted as informing about deserving punishment, not its occurrence.
C) Because God is not bound by threats, but He is bound by promises.
D) Because promises are from God's mercy and threats from His justice.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari defense of the promise-threat distinction relies on interpreting threats as about deserving punishment, not its occurrence, and grounding the obligation to keep promises in the religious certainty that God does not lie.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a threat in the Quran, such as 'Whoever does evil will be recompensed for it' (Q 4:123), do you immediately assume it will happen to you exactly as stated? How does this lesson's distinction between deserving and actual occurrence change your fear and hope in God's judgment?
?
Is there a promise from God that you have doubted because you felt unworthy? Reflect: if God's promises are not like human promises but are based on His faithfulness and your position, how can you hold onto His word more firmly today?
?
If God's threats are not absolute predictions but declarations of what you deserve, then your pardon depends on His grace, not your merit. Does this free you from despair or make you complacent? Which reaction does your soul lean toward, and what commitment can you make to respond rightly?

The First Objection: Finite Bodily Power
States consensus on eternal punishment and refutes first objection about finite power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic consensus on eternal punishment and the first rational objection based on the finitude of bodily power, along with its refutation.

Core Idea
Bodily power's finitude does not necessitate an end to punishment.

This lesson begins with the Islamic consensus that disbelievers will be punished eternally. Then it presents the first objection raised by a group outside Islam: since bodily power is finite, eventually the body must perish and sensation end, making punishment impossible. The answer is straightforward: the finitude of bodily power is denied, and the arguments for its finitude have already been refuted.

Guided Path
1
Read the consensus statement
Start by reading the assertion of consensus on eternal punishment for disbelievers. Notice that it covers all disbelievers regardless of their level of effort or obstinacy.
Consensus is a key source of authority in Islamic theology.
2
Understand the first objection
Identify the logical chain: bodily power is finite → it perishes → life and sensation end → punishment is inconceivable. The same applies to paradise.
This objection uses a premise already discussed earlier in the text.
3
See the answer's strategy
The answer simply denies the finitude of bodily power and refers to a prior refutation of the arguments for finitude. No new argument is given here.
The author assumes the reader has already accepted the refutation of finitude.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أجمع المسلمون على أن الكفار مخلدون في النار أبداً لا ينقطع عذابهم ) سواء بالغوا في الاجتهاد والنظر في معجزة الأنبياء ولم يهتدوا ، أو علموا نبوتهم وعاندوا أو تكاسلوا ( وأنكره ) أي تخليدهم في النار (طائفة ) خارجة عن الملة الإسلامية لوجوه : الأول : أن القوة الجسمانية كما تقدم متناهية ) في العدة والمدة ( فلا بد من فنائها ، وإذا فنيت قوة الحياة وما يتبعها من الحس والحركة ولم يبق إحساس فلا يتصور عذاب وهذه الشبهة بعينها جارية في انقطاع نعيم أهل الجنة ( الجواب : منع تناهيها وقد مر فساد ما يتمسك به في إثبات ذلك التناهي. (الثاني: ) من تلك الوجوه ( دوام الإحراق بقاء الحياة خروج عن قضية العقل . الجواب : هذا بناء على اعتبار ( شرط البنية واعتدال المزاج ) في الحياة ( و ) نحن لا نقول به بل هي ) أي الحياة (بخلق الله تعالى وقد يخلقها دائماً أبداً أو يخلق في الحي قوة لا يخرب معها بنيته بالنار) مع كونه متأذياً بها ( كما خلقها في السمندر ) مع عدم التأذي بها، (وهو حيوان مأواه النار و ... قوله : ( الثاني من تلك الوجوه إلخ) حكي أن واحداً من منكري الحشر أورد هذه الشبهة على الأستاذ أبي إسحاق الإسفرائني فأجاب أن مثل هذه الحالة موجودة فيما بيننا، وذلك لأن الأطعمة الغليظة تنطبخ بحرارة المعدة ويتجزى فيها بحيث لا يحصل مثل ذلك الانطباخ إذا جعل في القدر، والطبخ إنما يكون بالحرارة فدل ذلك على أن حرارة المعدة أقوى من حرارة القدر التي تغلي، ثم إنا لا نتألم بهذه الحرارة فإن جاز أن لا يكون الحرارة القوية مؤلمة فلان يجوز بقاء الحياة بها أولى .
Second: Muslims are unanimously agreed that the disbelievers will be eternally in the Fire, forever, their punishment will never be cut off—whether they exhausted themselves in effort and reflection upon the miracles of the prophets and did not find guidance, or they knew their prophethood and obstinately opposed, or were lazy. And a group outside the Islamic community denied it (i.e., their eternal punishment) due to some arguments: The first: That bodily power, as previously mentioned, is finite in number and duration, so it inevitably perishes. When the power of life and what follows from it of sensation and movement perishes, and no feeling remains, then punishment is inconceivable. This same doubt also applies to the cessation of the bliss of the people of Paradise. The answer: Denial of its finitude, and the unsoundness of what is clung to in proving that finitude has already been mentioned. Second: Among those arguments: The permanence of burning while life continues is a departure from the dictate of reason. The answer: This is based on considering the condition of structure and balanced temperament for life, but we do not say that. Rather, it (i.e., life) is by God's creation, and He may create it eternally forever, or He may create in the living being a power that does not destroy its structure with fire, even while it is pained by it—just as He created it in the salamander, which is not pained by it. And it is an animal whose home is the fire... His saying: (The second of those arguments, etc.) It is narrated that one of the deniers of resurrection posed this doubt to the master Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāʾīnī, and he answered that such a state exists among us: for coarse foods are cooked by the heat of the stomach and break down such that the same cooking does not occur if placed in a pot. Cooking is only by heat, so this indicates that the heat of the stomach is stronger than the heat of the pot that boils. Yet we do not feel pain from this heat. Since it is possible that strong heat is not painful, then the possibility of life persisting in it is even more likely.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage affirms the Islamic consensus on the eternal punishment of disbelievers in Hellfire, then addresses two rational objections raised by a group outside Islam. First objection: Bodily power is finite, so it must eventually perish; without life and sensation, punishment is impossible. The same doubt applies to Paradise. The answer simply denies the finitude of bodily power, citing that the arguments for finitude have already been refuted. Second objection: It is irrational to say that burning can continue while life remains. The answer refutes the assumption that life requires a specific bodily structure and balanced temperament (mizāj). Rather, life is directly created by God, and He can sustain it eternally even in fire. God can create a power in the living being that prevents the fire from destroying its structure, while still causing pain. The example of the salamander—a creature that lives in fire without being harmed—is given as an analogy. Then a story is related: a denier of resurrection posed this objection to Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāʾīnī, who replied that this phenomenon is observable: strong heat (like gastric heat) can cook food without causing pain to the body. If it is possible for strong heat not to be painful, then the possibility of life continuing in fire is even more plausible.

Key Terms
إجماع
خلود
قوة جسمانية
تناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection proves that punishment cannot be eternal because bodies must eventually wear out.
Correction: The answer denies that bodily power is necessarily finite; the arguments for finitude have already been refuted.

Checkpoint
How does the text answer the first objection about finite bodily power?
A) It admits finitude but says punishment continues in another form.
B) It denies the finitude of bodily power and references a prior refutation.
C) It says punishment is only spiritual after the body perishes.
D) It argues that God renews the body each moment.

Key Takeaway
The eternity of punishment is defended by denying the necessary finitude of bodily power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the reality of eternal punishment, does your heart tremble at God's justice, or do you find yourself secretly wishing it were not true? Which do you honor more—your finite sense of fairness or God's infinite wisdom? Today, in prayer, ask Him to align your heart with His decree.
?
The objection assumes that what is true in this world must be true in the next. Where in your life are you assuming that God's power is limited by your experience of how things work? Will you repent of that limit and trust His word instead of your reasoning?
?
The answer denies a premise the objector held as obvious. Are you clinging to a 'reasonable' doubt about any matter of the unseen—perhaps the reality of hell or the resurrection—because it doesn't fit your logic? Choose now: will you surrender that doubt, or will you let it lead you away from the truth?

The Second Objection: Life in Fire is Irrational
Refutes the second objection about permanence of burning with life and provides a story.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that permanent burning while alive is contrary to reason, and the answer which affirms that life is directly created by God and can be sustained in fire, as illustrated by the salamander and the stomach heat analogy.

Core Idea
God's power can sustain life and sensation even in fire without destroying the body.

The second objection claims that it is irrational for burning to continue while life remains. The answer refutes the assumption that life requires a specific bodily structure and balanced temperament. Instead, life is a direct creation of God, and He can preserve it in fire while still causing pain. A story about al-Isfarāʾīnī illustrates this with the analogy of strong stomach heat that does not cause pain.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
Identify the claim: permanent burning while life continues is against reason. This objection assumes life requires a specific bodily constitution.
2
Understand the answer's refutation
The answer rejects the condition of structure and balanced temperament. Life is directly created by God, and He can sustain it eternally in fire.
This emphasizes God's absolute creative power.
3
Reflect on the salamander example
God created a creature (salamander) that lives in fire without being harmed. This shows it is possible to preserve life in fire.
The salamander is pained? The text says 'without being pained' for the salamander, but for disbelievers they are pained.
4
Examine the story of al-Isfarāʾīnī
A denier of resurrection posed this objection to the scholar. He replied with an analogy: stomach heat cooks food but does not cause pain, so strong heat can be non-painful, making life in fire even more plausible.
This is an argument from analogy (qiyās).

The Fate of Children of Disbelievers
Discussion of three views on children of disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main scholarly positions on the eschatological fate of the children of disbelievers and the reasoning behind each view.

Core Idea
Three views exist: Hell, servants of Paradise, or knowledge-based judgment.

The fate of innocent children who die before reaching maturity is a profound theological question. This passage presents the major Sunni, Mu'tazilite, and a synthesizing opinion on whether the children of disbelievers enter Paradise or Hell. Understanding these positions clarifies broader principles of divine justice and scriptural interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source text once, then the literal translation. Notice the different opinions presented: majority, Mu'tazila, and an intermediate view.
The passage moves from the majority view to the Mu'tazilite objection and then to a reconciling position.
2
Identify the three views
Distinguish: (1) Majority: children of disbelievers are in Hell, based on general texts and a hadith. (2) Mu'tazila: they are servants of Paradise, based on justice and Quran 6:164. (3) Third view: fate depends on God's foreknowledge of what they would have done.
The third view is presented as a possible compromise.
3
Analyze the reasoning
Examine the proof for each view. Note how the majority uses general texts (ʿumūmāt) and a specific hadith. The Mu'tazila appeal to justice and a clear Quranic verse. The third view tries to harmonize the hadiths.
The author of the sharh seems to favor the third view as it combines the traditions.
4
Reflect on the key objection
The passage ends by rejecting the inclusion of children under general texts about disbelievers. Why does it say worldly rulings do not necessitate afterlife rulings?
This distinction between worldly legal status and eschatological fate is crucial.
5
Consider the underlying principles
Think about how each view reflects a different emphasis: on textual authority (majority), on rational justice (Mu'tazila), or on divine omniscience (third view). Which approach seems most consistent?
The passage invites reflection on how to balance scripture and reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( على أن الكفار مخلدون في النار أبداً واختلف في أطفالهم والأكثرون على أنهم في النار أيضا لدخولهم في العمومات ولما روي أن خديجة سأل النبي عن أطفال الكفار فقال النبي : في النار وقالت المعتزلة هم خدم أهل الجنة لأن تعذيب من لا جرم له ظلم ولقوله تعالى: ﴿ ولا تزر وازرة وزر أخرى ﴾ [الأنعام: ١٦٤ ] ، وقيل : من علم الله تعالى منهم الإيمان على تقدير البلوغ فهو في الجنة، ومن علم منه الكفر ، ففي النار، وقد يختار المذهب الأخير لأنه جامع للحديثين، وأما دخولهم تحت العمومات الواردة في حق الكفار فممنوع لأنهم ليسوا بكافرين وإعطاءهم حكم الكافرين في الدنيا لتبعية أبويهم لا يقتضي تبعيتهم إياهم في الخلود في النار .
His saying: (Upon that the disbelievers are eternally in the Fire forever) and there is disagreement about their children. The majority hold that they are also in the Fire because they are included under the general texts and because it was narrated that Khadījah asked the Prophet about the children of disbelievers and the Prophet said: 'In the Fire.' The Mu'tazila said: They are servants of the people of Paradise, because punishing one who has no sin is injustice, and due to His saying: 'And no bearer of burdens will bear the burden of another' (Q 6:164). And it was said: Whoever among them God knows would have believed had they reached maturity is in Paradise, and whoever He knows would have disbelieved is in the Fire. The latter view may be chosen because it combines the two hadiths. As for their inclusion under the general texts regarding disbelievers, it is prevented because they are not disbelievers, and giving them the ruling of disbelievers in this world due to following their parents does not necessitate their following them in eternal punishment in the Fire.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage discusses the eschatological fate of the children of disbelievers. It begins by noting that there is disagreement on the matter. The majority opinion is that these children will be in Hellfire, relying on two proofs: (1) they are included under the general texts that speak of punishment for disbelievers, and (2) a hadith in which the Prophet told Khadījah that the children of polytheists are in the Fire. The Mu'tazila, however, hold that these children will be servants of the people of Paradise. Their reasoning is that punishing someone who has committed no sin would be unjust, and they cite the Quranic verse 'No bearer of burdens will bear the burden of another' (6:164). A third view is mentioned: God knows which children would have believed had they lived to maturity—those go to Paradise; those He knows would have disbelieved go to Hell. The commentator notes that this last view may be preferred because it harmonizes the two hadith traditions (one indicating punishment, the other indicating innocence). Finally, the commentary rejects the inclusion of these children under the general verses about disbelievers, because they are not disbelievers. Their worldly legal status follows their parents, but that does not mean they follow them in eternal punishment.

Key Terms
أطفال الكفار
خلود
معتزلة
خدم أهل الجنة
تبعية
ظلم
العمومات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The majority view is unjust because it punishes innocent children.
Correction: The passage clarifies that worldly rulings do not necessitate afterlife fate, and the children are not disbelievers; their fate is based on divine knowledge, not mere parentage.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazilite view on the children of disbelievers?
A) They are in Paradise.
B) They are in Hell.
C) They are servants of the people of Paradise.
D) They are in a state of limbo.

Key Takeaway
The eschatological fate of children of disbelievers is disputed, with views ranging from Hell to Paradise service to a knowledge-based judgment, and the commentator favors the synthesizing view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the majority view that children of disbelievers may be in Hell, does it stir discomfort in your heart? How do you reconcile that feeling with your trust in God's perfect justice? Will you choose to surrender your own sense of fairness to His wisdom, even when it remains mysterious?
?
The commentator argues that worldly legal rulings do not determine afterlife fate. Think of someone you have silently judged because of their background or upbringing. Are you willing to stop assigning eternal labels based on external signs, and instead leave their ultimate fate to the One who knows what they would have done?
?
If you were to die tonight, what would God's knowledge of your potential choices say about your faith? Are you living in a way that when God looks at your heart, He sees unwavering belief? Let this question push you to examine your intentions and actions today.

The Muslim Consensus on Eternal Punishment
Al-Isfahani states the consensus on eternal punishment and its deniers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the unanimous Muslim position that disbelievers suffer eternal punishment in Hell, regardless of their level of effort or intent, and that this belief is a defining creedal point.

Core Idea
Eternal punishment for disbelievers is a matter of consensus, not debate.

Al-Isfahani opens by affirming the consensus of the Muslim community on the eternal punishment of disbelievers. This consensus is foundational to Islamic theology, and he also notes that a dissenting group exists outside the fold of Islam. This lesson focuses on the statement of consensus and the categories of disbelievers it covers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the categorical language and the mention of a dissenting group.
2
Identify the three types of disbelievers
The passage lists three subtypes of disbelievers: those who earnestly sought truth but failed, those who knew and rejected obstinately, and those who were negligent. Note that all are included in the consensus.
This categorization shows the universal scope of the punishment.
3
Note the denial by a deviant group
Observe that Al-Isfahani explicitly states that a group outside Islam denied this eternity. This emphasizes the creedal importance of the consensus.
4
Reflect on the role of consensus
Consider why the author leads with consensus (ijma) in a theological argument. Consensus serves as a source of authority in Islamic thought.
This is a typical method in kalam to establish a foundational belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجمع المسلمون على أن الكفار مخلدون في النار أبداً لا ينقطع عذابهم) سواء بالغوا في الاجتهاد والنظر في معجزة الأنبياء ولم يهتدوا ، أو علموا نبوتهم وعاندوا أو تكاسلوا ( وأنكره) أي تخليدهم في النار (طائفة ) خارجة عن الملة الإسلامية لوجوه : الأول : أن القوة الجسمانية كما تقدم متناهية) في العدة والمدة ( فلا بد من فنائها ، وإذا فنيت قوة الحياة وما يتبعها من الحس والحركة ولم يبق إحساس فلا يتصور عذاب وهذه الشبهة بعينها جارية في انقطاع نعيم أهل الجنة ( الجواب : منع تناهيها وقد مر فساد ما يتمسك به في إثبات ذلك التناهي.

The answer: denial of its finitude, and the corruption of what is relied upon to prove that finitude has already passed.

Refuting the First Objection: Finite Strength
Al-Isfahani presents the first objection and its rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to eternal punishment—that bodily strength is finite and thus sensation ends—and its concise refutation by denying the premise of finitude.

Core Idea
The finitude of bodily strength is not a proven fact, so the objection collapses.

Having stated the consensus, Al-Isfahani now presents the first rational objection raised by those who deny eternal punishment. The objection argues that since bodily strength is finite, life and sensation must eventually end, making punishment impossible. The author then gives a brief answer, denying the very claim of finitude. This lesson unpacks the logical structure of the objection and its response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice that the objection is presented as a logical chain, and the answer is a direct denial of its premise.
2
Understand the premise
The objection begins with the claim that bodily strength is finite (متناهية). Identify this premise and its basis (already established earlier, according to the text).
The phrase 'as has preceded' indicates prior discussion.
3
Trace the conclusion
From the finitude of strength, the objection concludes that eventually life strength perishes, leading to loss of sensation, so no punishment can be felt.
The same logic would also apply to the bliss of Paradise.
4
Analyze the answer
The answer does not engage with details but simply denies the original claim of finitude, asserting that its proof has already been refuted.
This is a typical method in kalam: rebutting the premise of an opponent.
5
Connect to the broader argument
Understand that this answer relies on a previous discussion; the objection is effectively neutralized by an earlier refutation.

Source Text

Children of Disbelievers: Three Opinions
Three opinions on children of disbelievers, with author's preference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main opinions on the fate of children of disbelievers and the reasoning behind each, including the author's preferred view.

Core Idea
The fate of innocent children poses a theological dilemma: justice vs. textual evidence.

In this lesson, we examine a controversial topic: what happens to the children of disbelievers who die before reaching maturity? The passage presents three distinct views, from the majority who place them in Hell, to the Mu'tazila who make them servants in Paradise, to a nuanced position based on divine foreknowledge. We will see how each side uses textual and rational arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Get a sense of the different opinions presented.
Notice the structure: first the majority, then Mu'tazila, then a third opinion, and finally a rebuttal.
2
Identify the three opinions
List the three opinions: (1) Majority: children are in the Fire. (2) Mu'tazila: they are servants of Paradise. (3) A middle view: their fate depends on what Allah knows they would have done.
Each opinion has its own evidence.
3
Examine the evidence for each
For the majority: general texts (عمومات) and a hadith from Khadija. For Mu'tazila: rational argument (no punishment without sin) and Quran 6:164. For the third: it reconciles the hadiths.
The author says the last opinion is preferred.
4
Understand the author's rebuttal
The author argues that children are not disbelievers, so general texts about disbelievers do not apply. Their worldly ruling as followers does not mean eternal punishment.
This is a key theological distinction.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why this issue matters: it touches on divine justice, mercy, and the interpretation of texts.
The lesson shows that even within Sunni thought, there is room for disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( على أن الكفار مخلدون في النار أبداً واختلف في أطفالهم والأكثرون على أنهم في النار أيضا لدخولهم في العمومات ولما روي أن خديجة سأل النبي عن أطفال الكفار فقال النبي : في النار وقالت المعتزلة هم خدم أهل الجنة لأن تعذيب من لا جرم له ظلم ولقوله تعالى: ﴿ ولا تزر وازرة وزر أخرى ﴾ [الأنعام: ١٦٤] ، وقيل : من علم الله تعالى منهم الإيمان على تقدير البلوغ فهو في الجنة، ومن علم منه الكفر ، ففي النار، وقد يختار المذهب الأخير لأنه جامع للحديثين، وأما دخولهم تحت العمومات الواردة في حق الكفار فممنوع لأنهم ليسوا بكافرين وإعطاءهم حكم الكافرين في الدنيا لتبعية أبويهم لا يقتضي تبعيتهم إياهم في الخلود في النار.
His statement: 'That the disbelievers are eternally in the Fire forever' and there is disagreement regarding their children. The majority hold that they are also in the Fire because they are included in the general [texts] and because of the narration that Khadija asked the Prophet about the children of the disbelievers, and the Prophet said: 'In the Fire.' The Mu'tazila said: They are servants of the people of Paradise because punishing one who has no sin is injustice, and because of His statement: 'And no bearer of burdens will bear the burden of another' [Quran 6:164]. And it was said: Whoever among them Allah knows would have believed if they had reached maturity is in Paradise, and whoever He knows would have disbelieved is in the Fire. And the last opinion may be chosen because it combines the two hadiths. As for their inclusion under the general texts concerning the disbelievers, it is rejected because they are not disbelievers, and giving them the ruling of disbelievers in this world due to following their parents does not necessitate that they follow them in eternity in the Fire.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the fate of children of disbelievers. It reports disagreement: the majority opinion holds they are in Hell, based on general texts and a hadith from Khadija. The Mu'tazila argue they are servants in Paradise, since punishing the innocent is unjust, citing Quran 6:164. A third view distinguishes based on what Allah foreknows they would have done if they had reached maturity. The author indicates the last view is preferable as it reconciles the evidence, and argues that inclusion in general texts is invalid because children are not disbelievers, and their worldly ruling as followers of parents does not transfer to eternal punishment.

Key Terms
أطفال الكفار
خدم أهل الجنة
العمومات
التبعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fate of children of disbelievers is the same as their parents.
Correction: The passage shows disagreement and argues that children are not disbelievers themselves, so their fate is distinct. The author prefers a nuanced view based on divine foreknowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the opinion of the Mu'tazila regarding the children of disbelievers?
A) They are in Hell like their parents.
B) They are servants of the people of Paradise.
C) Their fate is unknown and only Allah knows.
D) They are all in Paradise.

Key Takeaway
The fate of children of disbelievers is a disputed issue; the strongest opinion distinguishes based on divine foreknowledge and rejects automatic inclusion in general texts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refutation of al-Jahiz and al-Anbari on Excused Unbelievers
Refutation of al-Jahiz and al-Anbari's claim that sincere seekers are excused.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that sincere seekers of truth are excused from eternal punishment is rejected by the Quran, Sunnah, and early consensus.

Core Idea
Sincere effort does not excuse disbelief; scripture and consensus confirm eternal punishment for all who disbelieve.

This passage presents a controversial view by al-Jahiz and al-Anbari, who argue that those who earnestly seek truth but fail to find it are excused from eternal punishment. The author refutes this by invoking the Quran, Sunnah, and early consensus, and by pointing to the reality of the disbelievers at the time of the Prophet. Understanding this debate clarifies the Islamic position on accountability and the role of divine sealing of hearts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the distinction made by al-Jahiz and al-Anbari between obstinate/negligent disbelievers and those who exerted effort.
Pay attention to the terms 'muʿānid', 'muqṣir', and 'mubāligh fī ijṭihādihī'.
2
Identify the two views
The passage presents two opposing views: first, the view of al-Jahiz and al-Anbari (excusing the sincere seeker), and second, the author's refutation based on scripture and consensus.
3
Analyze the refutation
The author argues that the Book, Sunnah, and consensus rebut this distinction. They cite that early disbelievers were not all obstinate; many believed falsehood after effort or remained in doubt, yet were judged eternally.
Note the key phrase 'Allah sealed their hearts' – this is a theological concept.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this refutation is important: it upholds that divine knowledge of reality and the sealing of hearts are beyond human judgment. The distinction introduced by al-Jahiz and al-Anbari is an innovation.
This connects to the broader Islamic principle that religious truth is known through revelation, not just reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الجاحظ و عبد الله بن الحسن العنبري هذا الذي ذكرناه من دوام العذاب إنما هو في حق الكافر المعاند والمقصر، وأما المبالغ في اجتهاده إذا لم يهتد للإسلام ولم تلح له دلائل الحق فمعذور وعذابه منقطع، وكيف يكلف مثل هذا الشخص بما ليس في وسعه من تصديق النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، وكيف يعذب بما لم يقع فيه تقصير من قبله. واعلم أن الكتاب والسنة والإجماع المنعقد قبل ظهور المخالفين يبطل ذلك بل نقول : هو مخالف لما علم من الدين ضرورة، إذ يعلم قطعاً أن كفار عهد الرسول الذين قتلوا وحكم بخلودهم في النار لم يكونوا عن آخرهم معاندين بل منهم من يعتقد الكفر بعد بذل المجهود، ومنهم من بقي على الشك بعد إفراغ الوسع لكن الله طبع على قلوبهم ولم يشرح صدورهم للإسلام فلم يهتدوا إلى حقيقته، ولم أحد قبل الخالفين هذا الفرق الذي ذكره الجاحظ والعنبري.
Al-Jahiz and 'Abdullah ibn al-Hasan al-'Anbari said: That which we have mentioned of the perpetual punishment is only concerning the obstinate and negligent disbeliever. As for the one who exerts his utmost effort in investigation if he does not attain guidance to Islam and the proofs of truth are not clear to him, then he is excused and his punishment is cut off. How can such a person be charged with what is beyond his capacity, i.e., believing in the Prophet, peace be upon him? And how can he be punished for a shortcoming that did not occur on his part? Know that the Book, the Sunnah, and the consensus that was established before the appearance of the opponents rebut that. Rather, we say: It is contrary to what is known necessarily from religion, for it is known with certainty that the disbelievers of the time of the Messenger, who were killed and judged to be eternally in the Fire, were not all obstinate; rather, among them were those who believed in disbelief after exerting effort, and among them were those who remained in doubt after exhausting their capacity, but Allah sealed their hearts and did not open their breasts to Islam, so they did not attain its reality. And no one before the opponents made this distinction that al-Jahiz and al-'Anbari mentioned.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage presents the view of al-Jahiz and al-'Anbari, who distinguish between two types of unbelievers: the obstinate and negligent, who deserve eternal punishment, and those who sincerely exerted effort but did not find truth, who are excused and their punishment is temporary. The author refutes this by stating that it contradicts the Quran, Sunnah, and early consensus. He argues that many of the disbelievers in the Prophet's time were not obstinate; some believed in disbelief after effort, others remained in doubt, yet they were judged as eternally in Hell. Therefore, this distinction is a later innovation.

Key Terms
المعاند
المقصر
المبالغ في الاجتهاد
المعذور
الطبع على القلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a person sincerely seeks truth but does not find it, they are excused from punishment.
Correction: The passage shows that many disbelievers in the Prophet's time sincerely sought truth but were still judged eternally, because Allah sealed their hearts. The distinction is an innovation not supported by early consensus.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the distinction made by al-Jahiz and al-'Anbari?
A) Because it contradicts the Quran, Sunnah, and early consensus.
B) Because all unbelievers are obstinate.
C) Because effort alone cannot lead to truth.
D) Because the distinction is too lenient.

Key Takeaway
The claim that sincere seekers are excused from eternal punishment is an innovation rejected by the Quran, Sunnah, consensus, and the reality of the Prophet's contemporaries.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you still consider them excused, or will you pray for their guidance knowing that only Allah opens hearts?
?

How can you ensure your understanding of Allah's justice is shaped by the Quran and Sunnah rather than your own reasoning?

Believing Sinners Are Not Eternally Punished
Statement and evidence for non-eternal punishment of sinful believers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that believers who commit major sins are not eternally punished in Hell, based on the Quranic promise that every atom's weight of good will be seen.

Core Idea
A believer's faith outweighs any sin for eternal fate.

This lesson addresses a crucial distinction in Islamic eschatology: only disbelief leads to eternal punishment, not sins committed by believers. The author cites a powerful Quranic verse to support this, reminding us that even the smallest good—like faith—will be rewarded. Let's see how he argues this point.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without worry about missing parts. Focus on the core claim and the verse.
The passage is slightly truncated but the main idea is clear.
2
Understand the key claim
Identify the statement: 'Non-disbeliever sinners are not eternally in the Fire.' Notice that this includes committers of major sins who are still believers.
3
Examine the evidence
Read the Quranic verse (99:7) and see how it is used as proof. The verse promises reward for any good deed, even an atom's weight. The author points out that a sinner's faith is good, so they will see it.
This implies that faith saves from eternal punishment.
4
Consider the discussion started
The passage begins discussing whether seeing the good occurs before entering Hell or in Hell as a reduction. This is part of a larger argument not fully shown here.
Even incomplete, the main doctrine is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث : غير الكفار من العصاة ومرتكبي الكبائر لا يخلد في النار لقوله تعالى : ﴿ فمن يعمل مثقال ذرة خيراً يره ﴾ [الزلزلة : ٧]، ولا شك أن مرتكب الكبيرة قد عمل خيراً هو إيمانه، فأما أن يكون ذلك أي رؤيته للخير، قبل دخول النار ثم ... قوله : ( فأما أن يكون ذلك قبل دخول النار إلخ) قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يرى في النار تخفيف
The third topic: Other than the disbelievers, i.e., the disobedient and committers of major sins, are not eternally in the Fire, due to His statement: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' [Quran 99:7]. There is no doubt that the committer of a major sin has done good, which is his faith. Then whether that, i.e., his seeing the good, is before entering the Fire or then... His statement: (As for that being before entering the Fire, etc.) It was said: Why is it not permissible that he see it in the Fire as a reduction?
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage introduces the third main topic of the section: that believers who commit major sins are not eternally punished in Hell. The evidence is the Quranic verse promising that everyone will see any good they have done, even an atom's weight. The author notes that even a major sinner has the good of faith. The passage then begins to discuss whether this seeing of good occurs before entering Hell or after, and a suggestion is made that it could be a reduction of punishment while in the Fire. The passage is incomplete, but the principle is clear: sinful believers are not eternally in Hell.

Key Terms
العصاة
مرتكبي الكبائر
التخليد
مثقال ذرة
الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any sin, even from a believer, leads to eternal punishment in Hell.
Correction: Only disbelief (kufr) leads to eternal punishment. Believers who commit sins, including major sins, are eventually saved due to their faith, as promised in Quran 99:7.

Checkpoint
What evidence is given that sinful believers are not eternally in Hell?
A) The verse 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it.'
B) The consensus of the companions.
C) The hadith of intercession.
D) Rational argument about God's mercy.

Key Takeaway
Believing sinners are not eternally punished because their faith is a good deed that will be rewarded, as stated in the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you take comfort in your faith as a safety net, or does the mercy of Allah in this verse drive you to repent more earnestly? What would change if you truly believed that your faith will be rewarded despite your shortcomings?
?
This lesson teaches that a single good deed—faith—outweighs all sins for eternal fate. Does knowing this make you feel entitled to Paradise, or does it deepen your gratitude and motivate you to obey more? How can you guard against ingratitude and complacency?
?
If you were to stand before Allah tomorrow, would your faith be enough for you to hope for mercy? Or have you placed your trust in your deeds alone? Based on Quran 99:7, what one change can you make today to ensure your faith is alive and visible in your daily actions?
