Refutation of Jahiz and 'Anbari on Punishment
Author refutes the view that only obstinate disbelievers face eternal punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author rejects the distinction between obstinate and sincere disbelievers, and how he uses scripture, consensus, and historical evidence to argue that all disbelievers are eternally punished.

Core Idea
Sincere ignorance does not excuse from eternal punishment.

In this passage, the author confronts a proposal by Jahiz and 'Anbari that eternal punishment applies only to obstinate or negligent disbelievers, not to those who earnestly seek truth but fail. The author argues that this distinction contradicts the Qur'an, Sunnah, and consensus, and is refuted by the historical fact that many disbelievers in the Prophet's time were not obstinate yet were condemned.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage to understand Jahiz and 'Anbari's claim
Start by reading the Arabic and translation, noting the distinction they make between the obstinate disbeliever and the sincere seeker.
2
Identify the author's primary sources of refutation
Look for how the author invokes the Book, Sunnah, and prior consensus to reject the distinction.
The author says these sources invalidate the claim.
3
Analyze the historical argument
The author points to the example of disbelievers in the Prophet's era: many were not obstinate but were still condemned to eternal fire.
This is a key empirical counterexample.
4
Understand the theological lesson
Reflect on the principle that knowledge of truth is not solely dependent on personal effort—God may seal hearts. Thus, the distinction fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الجاحظ و عبد الله بن الحسن العنبري: هذا الذي ذكرناه من دوام العذاب إنما هو في حق الكافر المعاند والمقصر، وأما المبالغ في اجتهاده إذا لم يهتد للإسلام ولم تلح له دلائل الحق فمعذور وعذابه منقطع. وكيف يكلف مثل هذا الشخص بما ليس في وسعه من تصديق النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، وكيف يعذب بما لم يقع فيه تقصير من قبله. واعلم أن الكتاب والسنة والإجماع المنعقد قبل ظهور المخالفين يبطل ذلك بل نقول: هو مخالف لما علم من الدين ضرورة، إذ يعلم قطعاً أن كفار عهد الرسول الذين قتلوا وحكم بخلودهم في النار لم يكونوا عن آخرهم معاندين بل منهم من يعتقد الكفر بعد بذل المجهود، ومنهم من بقي على الشك بعد إفراغ الوسع لكن الله طبع على قلوبهم ولم يشرح صدورهم للإسلام فلم يهتدوا إلى حقيقته، ولم أحد قبل الخالفين هذا الفرق الذي ذكره الجاحظ والعنبري.
Al-Jahiz and 'Abdullah ibn al-Hasan al-'Anbari said: This that we have mentioned of the eternity of punishment is only with respect to the obstinate disbeliever and the negligent. As for one who exerted utmost effort in his ijtihad but did not attain guidance to Islam and the proofs of truth were not clear to him, he is excused and his punishment is temporary. How can such a person be required to do what is beyond his capacity, namely believing the Prophet (peace be upon him)? And how can he be punished for something in which there was no negligence on his part? Know that the Book, the Sunnah, and the consensus that was established before the emergence of opponents invalidate that. Rather we say: It contradicts what is known necessarily from religion, for it is known with certainty that the disbelievers of the Prophet's era who were killed and judged to be eternally in fire were not all obstinate; rather among them were those who believed in disbelief after exerting effort, and among them were those who remained in doubt after exhausting their capacity, but God sealed their hearts and did not expand their breasts to Islam, so they did not attain its reality. And no one before the opponents made this distinction that al-Jahiz and al-'Anbari mentioned.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage refutes the distinction proposed by Jahiz and 'Anbari that only the obstinate and negligent disbeliever will be eternally punished, while the sincere seeker who fails to reach Islam is excused and punished only temporarily. The author counters that this distinction is invalidated by the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus. Historical facts show that not all disbelievers in the Prophet's time were obstinate; many believed in disbelief after sincere effort, and others remained in doubt despite exhausting their capacity. Thus the claim that such people are excused is false and contradicts what is necessarily known from religion.

Key Terms
المعاند
المقصر
المبالغ في اجتهاده
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sincere seekers of truth who fail to accept Islam are excused from eternal punishment.
Correction: The author argues that even sincere seekers are not excused; historical evidence shows many were condemned despite their effort.

Checkpoint
What is the main basis for the author's refutation of Jahiz and 'Anbari?
A) The Quran and Sunnah command forgiveness for all
B) Historical evidence that many disbelievers were not obstinate but still condemned
C) The principle that all people have equal capacity to believe
D) Consensus that punishment is eternal for all disbelievers

Key Takeaway
The author maintains that all disbelievers, regardless of their sincerity, face eternal punishment, as established by revelation and historical precedent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you truly seeking the truth with all your effort, or are you content with comfortable beliefs that spare you from the weight of conviction? The sincere seeker in the passage was not saved—his sincerity did not protect him. What if your own sincerity is misdirected?
?
The author says God sealed their hearts. Have you ever felt your heart hardened against a truth you knew you should accept? What are you doing to soften it before it is too late?
?
Jahiz and 'Anbari's view seems merciful because it excuses the sincere. But the author rejects it as innovation. Are you tempted to redefine Allah's justice to fit your own standards of fairness? Will you submit to His judgment even when it challenges your sense of mercy?

Non-Disbelieving Sinners Not Eternally Punished
Author proves that sinful believers are not eternally in Hell using Quranic verse and logical disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Muslim scholars hold that a believer who commits major sins will not remain in Hell forever, based on the Quranic verse about seeing even an atom's weight of good.

Core Idea
Faith is good; therefore, a sinful believer must eventually leave Hell.

In this short but decisive passage, the author presents a logical proof grounded in the Quran to settle a major theological question: do sinful believers face eternal punishment? The argument turns on a single verse (99:7) and the undeniable fact that faith itself is a good deed. By examining two possible timings for 'seeing' that good, the author shows that the only consistent conclusion is the sinner's eventual exit from Hell.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation slowly. Notice the concise argument structure.
2
Identify the key verse and its role
The passage quotes Quran 99:7. Understand that this verse promises that every good deed will be seen – i.e., rewarded. This is the foundation of the proof.
The verse is from Surah Az-Zalzalah.
3
Trace the logical disjunction
The author presents two possibilities for when the sinner 'sees' his good deed (his faith): before entering Hell or after exiting. Follow the reasoning that leads to rejecting the first and accepting the second.
The rejection of the first option is based on scholarly consensus.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Realize that the only remaining possibility is that the good deed is seen after exiting Hell, which directly proves that the sinner does leave Hell and is not eternally punished.
This conclusion aligns with the Sunni belief that faith saves a person from eternal damnation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث: غير الكفار من العصاة ومرتكبي الكبائر لا يخلد في النار لقوله تعالى: فمن يعمل مثقال ذرة خيراً يره. ولا شك أن مرتكب الكبيرة قد عمل خيراً هو إيمانه، فأما أن يكون ذلك أي رؤيته للخير قبل دخول النار ثم يدخل النار وهو باطل بالإجماع أو بعد خروجه عنها وفيه المطلوب وهو خروجه عن النار وعدم خلوده فيها.
The third discussion: Other than the disbelievers, the sinners and committers of major sins do not remain eternally in the Fire, because of the Almighty's saying: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' (99:7). There is no doubt that the committer of a major sin has done good, which is his faith. So either that seeing of the good occurs before entering the Fire, then he enters the Fire – but that is invalid by consensus – or it occurs after exiting it, and in that is the desired conclusion, namely his exit from the Fire and his not remaining eternally in it.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage provides a proof that sinful believers are not eternally damned. The proof relies on Quran 99:7: every good deed will be seen. The sinner has the good deed of faith. If that good is seen before entering Hell, he would not enter Hell, but that contradicts the fact that some sinners do enter Hell. Therefore, the seeing must occur after exiting Hell, which proves that he will eventually leave Hell. Thus, eternity in Hell for a sinful believer is impossible.

Key Terms
خلود
كبائر
الخير
الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' refers only to reward in this world, not the afterlife.
Correction: The author uses the verse in an afterlife context. The seeing must occur either before or after Hell, both eschatological scenarios. The consensus against seeing before entering Hell is based on the afterlife nature of the reward. Thus, the verse necessarily applies to the Hereafter.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does Quran 99:7 imply for a sinner who has faith?
A) He will not enter Hell at all
B) He must see his good deed before entering Hell
C) He must see his good deed after leaving Hell, proving eventual exit
D) The verse does not apply to sinners

Key Takeaway
Because faith is a good deed, Quran 99:7 guarantees that a sinful believer will eventually see its reward, which can only happen after exiting Hell, thus proving that believers are not eternally punished.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat the 'atom's weight' of faith in your heart as a sure guarantee of salvation, or do you secretly doubt its worth because of your sins? What would change if you took this proof as absolute truth today?
?
If this proof removes the fear of eternal punishment for believers, does it also remove your urgency to repent from major sins? How can you hold both the hope of eventual salvation and the fear of Allah's punishment without falling into complacency?
?
This proof relies on the consensus that reward cannot come before punishment. But in your own life, do you sometimes seek the reward of faith (peace, blessings) while avoiding the trial and purification that comes from accountability? What does that say about your trust in Allah's wisdom?

The Mu'tazila and Khawarij View of Ihbat
Author presents and rejects the Mu'tazila/Khawarij view on ihbat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila and Khawarij doctrine that a single major sin nullifies all good deeds, and the early refutation of this view.

Core Idea
One sin cannot wipe out a lifetime of obedience.

The discussion of ihbat (nullification) begins with the most extreme view: that a single major sin eliminates all previous good deeds. The author presents this claim and immediately points out its absurdity and contradiction with revealed texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the author's clear dismissal.
2
Identify the claim and its basis
The Mu'tazila build on the idea that punishment is deserved and incompatible with reward. See how they conclude that one major sin nullifies all.
The example of a lifetime worshipper drinking wine illustrates the severity.
3
Notice the author's refutation
The author says the view is manifestly corrupt because it cancels all good deeds and contradicts Quranic promises.
This sets the stage for the orthodox position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السابع: في إحباط الطاعات بالمعاصي. في الإحباط بنى المعتزلة على استحقاق العقاب ومنافاته للثواب، واستحقاقه إحباط الطاعات بالمعاصي ثم اختلفوا فقال جمهور المعتزلة والخوارج أيضاً بمعصية أي بكبيرة واحدة تحبط الطاعات حتى إن من عبد الله طول عمره ثم شرب جرعة خمر فهو كمن لم يعبده أبداً، ولا يخفى فساده لأنه إلغاء للطاعات بالكلية ومناف للعمومات الدالة على ثواب الإيمان والعمل الصالح. قال الآمدي: إذا اجتمع في المؤمن طاعات وزلات فإجماع أهل الحق من الأشاعرة وغيرهم أنه لا يجب على الله ثوابه ولا عقابه فإن أثابه فبفضله وإن عاقبه فبعدله، بل له إثابة العاصي وعقاب المطيع أيضاً. وذهبت المرجئة إلى أن الإيمان يحبط الزلات فلا عقاب على زلة مع الإيمان كما لا ثواب لطاعة مع الكفر. وقالت المعتزلة إن كبيرة واحدة تحبط ثواب جميع الطاعات وإن زادت على زلته. وذهب الجبائي وابنه إلى رعاية الكثرة في المحبط وزعما أن من زادت طاعاته على زلاته أحبطت عقاب زلاته وكفرتها، ومن زادت زلاته على طاعاته أحبطت ثواب طاعاته، ثم اختلفا فقال العذاب وتفاوت الدرجات، فالأولى التمسك بالإجماع قبل ظهور المخالف ويمكن أن يدفع بأن جزاء الإيمان هو الثواب بالإجماع ودار الثواب هي الجنة.
The seventh purpose: On nullification of obediences by sins. Regarding nullification, the Mu'tazila built upon the deserving of punishment and its incompatibility with reward, and its deserving nullifies obediences by sins. Then they differed: the majority of the Mu'tazila and the Khawarij also said that by one sin, i.e., one major sin, all obediences are nullified, so that whoever worshipped God his entire life and then drank a gulp of wine is like one who never worshipped Him at all. Its corruption is not hidden, because it is a cancellation of obediences entirely and contradicts the general texts indicating reward for faith and righteous action. Al-Amidi said: When in a believer there are acts of obedience and slips, the consensus of Ahl al-Haqq from the Ash'aris and others is that neither reward nor punishment is obligatory upon God; if He rewards, it is by His grace, and if He punishes, it is by His justice; rather He may reward the disobedient and punish the obedient as well. The Murji'a held that faith nullifies slips, so there is no punishment for a slip with faith, just as there is no reward for obedience with disbelief. The Mu'tazila said that one major sin nullifies the reward of all obediences, even if it exceeds the slip. Al-Jubba'i and his son adopted the consideration of quantity in nullification, claiming that if one's obediences outweigh one's slips, they nullify the punishment of the slips and expiate them, and if one's slips outweigh one's obediences, they nullify the reward of the obediences. Then they differed and said: punishment and differentiation of degrees. So the best is to adhere to the consensus before the emergence of opponents. And it can be refuted by saying that the reward of faith is by consensus, and the abode of reward is Paradise.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage introduces the doctrine of nullification (ihbat) and surveys the views of various theological groups. The Mu'tazila and Khawarij hold that a single major sin nullifies all former good deeds. The author quickly dismisses this as corrupt because it contradicts scriptural promises of reward. Al-Amidi states the Ash'ari consensus: God is not obligated to reward or punish; He acts by His will. The Murji'a hold that faith nullifies sins, so no punishment remains. The Mu'tazila insist one major sin destroys all reward. Al-Jubba'i and his son introduce a quantitative balance: good deeds can cancel punishment and vice versa. The passage ends by recommending adherence to the pre-opponent consensus and notes a possible objection that faith's reward is Paradise by consensus.

Key Terms
الإحباط
الطاعات
المعاصي
الكبائر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila and Khawarij, what happens to a person who worshipped all his life but commits one major sin?
A) He is still rewarded
B) His sins are forgiven
C) He becomes like one who never worshipped at all
D) His good deeds are halved

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila/Khawarij view that one major sin nullifies all good deeds is rejected because it contradicts scriptural promises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that one sin could wipe out a lifetime of worship, how would that change the way you live today? Would it lead to despair or recklessness? How does the author's rejection of this view free you from that false fear, and what does it demand from you instead?
?
Think of a time you committed a sin that made you feel all your good deeds were worthless. In light of this lesson, what is the truth about that feeling? Are you holding onto a Mu'tazila mindset even without knowing it? Commit to rejecting that despair and trusting in God's promise while still fearing His justice.
?
The Mu'tazila built their view on 'deserving punishment'. Do you view your relationship with God as a ledger of merits and demerits? How does this lesson call you to a deeper trust in God's mercy without neglecting accountability? Will you stop calculating your deeds and instead focus on sincere faith and action?

The Ash'ari and Murji'a Positions on Reward and Punishment
Author presents Ash'ari consensus and Murji'a view on reward/punishment.

Learning Objective
This lesson explains the Ash'ari consensus that God is not obligated to reward or punish, and the Murji'a view that faith nullifies all sins.

Core Idea
God's justice and grace are not bound by human expectations.

After dismissing the extreme nullification view, the author presents the orthodox Ash'ari position: God acts according to His will, not obligation. Then the Murji'a counter with the opposite extreme: faith erases all sins.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Slowly read the Arabic and English. Note the shift from 'قال الآمدي' as an authority.
2
Understand al-Amidi's statement of consensus
Al-Amidi says that Ahl al-Haqq agree that God is not obligated to reward or punish. He acts by grace or justice, and may even reward the disobedient and punish the obedient.
This directly opposes the Mu'tazila notion of obligation.
3
Contrast with the Murji'a view
The Murji'a hold that faith nullifies all slips, so no punishment remains with faith. Compare this to the Ash'ari position.
Both are extremes: Ash'ari middle path.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السابع: في إحباط الطاعات بالمعاصي. في الإحباط بنى المعتزلة على استحقاق العقاب ومنافاته للثواب، واستحقاقه إحباط الطاعات بالمعاصي ثم اختلفوا فقال جمهور المعتزلة والخوارج أيضاً بمعصية أي بكبيرة واحدة تحبط الطاعات حتى إن من عبد الله طول عمره ثم شرب جرعة خمر فهو كمن لم يعبده أبداً، ولا يخفى فساده لأنه إلغاء للطاعات بالكلية ومناف للعمومات الدالة على ثواب الإيمان والعمل الصالح. قال الآمدي: إذا اجتمع في المؤمن طاعات وزلات فإجماع أهل الحق من الأشاعرة وغيرهم أنه لا يجب على الله ثوابه ولا عقابه فإن أثابه فبفضله وإن عاقبه فبعدله، بل له إثابة العاصي وعقاب المطيع أيضاً. وذهبت المرجئة إلى أن الإيمان يحبط الزلات فلا عقاب على زلة مع الإيمان كما لا ثواب لطاعة مع الكفر. وقالت المعتزلة إن كبيرة واحدة تحبط ثواب جميع الطاعات وإن زادت على زلته. وذهب الجبائي وابنه إلى رعاية الكثرة في المحبط وزعما أن من زادت طاعاته على زلاته أحبطت عقاب زلاته وكفرتها، ومن زادت زلاته على طاعاته أحبطت ثواب طاعاته، ثم اختلفا فقال العذاب وتفاوت الدرجات، فالأولى التمسك بالإجماع قبل ظهور المخالف ويمكن أن يدفع بأن جزاء الإيمان هو الثواب بالإجماع ودار الثواب هي الجنة.
The seventh purpose: On nullification of obediences by sins. Regarding nullification, the Mu'tazila built upon the deserving of punishment and its incompatibility with reward, and its deserving nullifies obediences by sins. Then they differed: the majority of the Mu'tazila and the Khawarij also said that by one sin, i.e., one major sin, all obediences are nullified, so that whoever worshipped God his entire life and then drank a gulp of wine is like one who never worshipped Him at all. Its corruption is not hidden, because it is a cancellation of obediences entirely and contradicts the general texts indicating reward for faith and righteous action. Al-Amidi said: When in a believer there are acts of obedience and slips, the consensus of Ahl al-Haqq from the Ash'aris and others is that neither reward nor punishment is obligatory upon God; if He rewards, it is by His grace, and if He punishes, it is by His justice; rather He may reward the disobedient and punish the obedient as well. The Murji'a held that faith nullifies slips, so there is no punishment for a slip with faith, just as there is no reward for obedience with disbelief. The Mu'tazila said that one major sin nullifies the reward of all obediences, even if it exceeds the slip. Al-Jubba'i and his son adopted the consideration of quantity in nullification, claiming that if one's obediences outweigh one's slips, they nullify the punishment of the slips and expiate them, and if one's slips outweigh one's obediences, they nullify the reward of the obediences. Then they differed and said: punishment and differentiation of degrees. So the best is to adhere to the consensus before the emergence of opponents. And it can be refuted by saying that the reward of faith is by consensus, and the abode of reward is Paradise.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage introduces the doctrine of nullification (ihbat) and surveys the views of various theological groups. The Mu'tazila and Khawarij hold that a single major sin nullifies all former good deeds. The author quickly dismisses this as corrupt because it contradicts scriptural promises of reward. Al-Amidi states the Ash'ari consensus: God is not obligated to reward or punish; He acts by His will. The Murji'a hold that faith nullifies sins, so no punishment remains. The Mu'tazila insist one major sin destroys all reward. Al-Jubba'i and his son introduce a quantitative balance: good deeds can cancel punishment and vice versa. The passage ends by recommending adherence to the pre-opponent consensus and notes a possible objection that faith's reward is Paradise by consensus.

Key Terms
الإحباط
الطاعات
المعاصي
المرجئة
الأشاعرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Haqq as cited by al-Amidi, what is God's relation to reward and punishment?
A) God must reward the obedient
B) God must punish the disobedient
C) God is not obligated to reward or punish
D) Faith nullifies all punishment

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position frees God from any obligation to reward or punish, while the Murji'a faith-alone view is considered extreme.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your obedience does not obligate God to reward you, what truly motivates you to perform good deeds? Examine your heart: are you seeking reward, or are you serving God purely for His sake?
?
The Murji'a view that faith excuses all sins can be comforting. In what areas of your life have you perhaps excused your own sins by thinking 'I have faith, so it's fine'? How does this passage challenge that assumption?
?
God may punish the obedient and reward the disobedient. How does this truth affect your sense of security? Are you more focused on God's grace or on your own merit? What one change can you make today to rely more on His grace?

The Jubba'is' Quantitative Balance and Final Argument
Author presents Jubba'is' quantitative view and final consensus argument.

Learning Objective
This lesson covers the quantitative approach of al-Jubba'i and his son, and the author's recommendation to adhere to earlier consensus.

Core Idea
Weighing deeds is for the afterlife, not for nullification.

The last view considered is that of al-Jubba'i and his son, who propose a balancing scale: good deeds can cancel punishment, and bad deeds can cancel reward. The author then advises holding to the consensus before these disputes arose.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin with the restated Mu'tazila view, then the Jubba'is' proposal, and finally the author's conclusion.
2
Understand the quantitative balance
Al-Jubba'i and his son say if good deeds outweigh sins, they nullify punishment; if sins outweigh, they nullify reward. Note their internal disagreement on the outcome.
This is a more nuanced version of ihbat.
3
Grasp the author's conclusion
The author recommends adhering to the consensus before opponents appeared, and notes that faith's reward is Paradise by consensus, refuting the idea of nullification.
This effectively ends the discussion on ihbat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السابع: في إحباط الطاعات بالمعاصي. في الإحباط بنى المعتزلة على استحقاق العقاب ومنافاته للثواب، واستحقاقه إحباط الطاعات بالمعاصي ثم اختلفوا فقال جمهور المعتزلة والخوارج أيضاً بمعصية أي بكبيرة واحدة تحبط الطاعات حتى إن من عبد الله طول عمره ثم شرب جرعة خمر فهو كمن لم يعبده أبداً، ولا يخفى فساده لأنه إلغاء للطاعات بالكلية ومناف للعمومات الدالة على ثواب الإيمان والعمل الصالح. قال الآمدي: إذا اجتمع في المؤمن طاعات وزلات فإجماع أهل الحق من الأشاعرة وغيرهم أنه لا يجب على الله ثوابه ولا عقابه فإن أثابه فبفضله وإن عاقبه فبعدله، بل له إثابة العاصي وعقاب المطيع أيضاً. وذهبت المرجئة إلى أن الإيمان يحبط الزلات فلا عقاب على زلة مع الإيمان كما لا ثواب لطاعة مع الكفر. وقالت المعتزلة إن كبيرة واحدة تحبط ثواب جميع الطاعات وإن زادت على زلته. وذهب الجبائي وابنه إلى رعاية الكثرة في المحبط وزعما أن من زادت طاعاته على زلاته أحبطت عقاب زلاته وكفرتها، ومن زادت زلاته على طاعاته أحبطت ثواب طاعاته، ثم اختلفا فقال العذاب وتفاوت الدرجات، فالأولى التمسك بالإجماع قبل ظهور المخالف ويمكن أن يدفع بأن جزاء الإيمان هو الثواب بالإجماع ودار الثواب هي الجنة.
The seventh purpose: On nullification of obediences by sins. Regarding nullification, the Mu'tazila built upon the deserving of punishment and its incompatibility with reward, and its deserving nullifies obediences by sins. Then they differed: the majority of the Mu'tazila and the Khawarij also said that by one sin, i.e., one major sin, all obediences are nullified, so that whoever worshipped God his entire life and then drank a gulp of wine is like one who never worshipped Him at all. Its corruption is not hidden, because it is a cancellation of obediences entirely and contradicts the general texts indicating reward for faith and righteous action. Al-Amidi said: When in a believer there are acts of obedience and slips, the consensus of Ahl al-Haqq from the Ash'aris and others is that neither reward nor punishment is obligatory upon God; if He rewards, it is by His grace, and if He punishes, it is by His justice; rather He may reward the disobedient and punish the obedient as well. The Murji'a held that faith nullifies slips, so there is no punishment for a slip with faith, just as there is no reward for obedience with disbelief. The Mu'tazila said that one major sin nullifies the reward of all obediences, even if it exceeds the slip. Al-Jubba'i and his son adopted the consideration of quantity in nullification, claiming that if one's obediences outweigh one's slips, they nullify the punishment of the slips and expiate them, and if one's slips outweigh one's obediences, they nullify the reward of the obediences. Then they differed and said: punishment and differentiation of degrees. So the best is to adhere to the consensus before the emergence of opponents. And it can be refuted by saying that the reward of faith is by consensus, and the abode of reward is Paradise.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage introduces the doctrine of nullification (ihbat) and surveys the views of various theological groups. The Mu'tazila and Khawarij hold that a single major sin nullifies all former good deeds. The author quickly dismisses this as corrupt because it contradicts scriptural promises of reward. Al-Amidi states the Ash'ari consensus: God is not obligated to reward or punish; He acts by His will. The Murji'a hold that faith nullifies sins, so no punishment remains. The Mu'tazila insist one major sin destroys all reward. Al-Jubba'i and his son introduce a quantitative balance: good deeds can cancel punishment and vice versa. The passage ends by recommending adherence to the pre-opponent consensus and notes a possible objection that faith's reward is Paradise by consensus.

Key Terms
الإحباط
الطاعات
المعاصي
الجبائي
الكثرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Jubba'i and his son, what happens if a person's good deeds outweigh his sins?
A) Sins are forgiven automatically
B) Good deeds are nullified
C) Punishment of sins is nullified and expiated
D) Both are cancelled

Key Takeaway
The quantitative approach of the Jubba'is is also set aside in favor of the earlier consensus that faith's reward is Paradise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own balance of good and bad deeds, do you tend to lean on your own calculation or on the promise of faith? What would change if you truly believed that the reward of faith is Paradise, regardless of your personal scale? Will you entrust your account to God's mercy today?
?
The Jubba'is' model seems logical and fair—so why does the author dismiss it as innovation? Are there comfortable 'logical' beliefs you hold about religion that might actually be innovations? Will you actively compare them to the consensus of the salaf?
?
If the reward of faith is Paradise, what does that mean for how you view your own slips and obediences? Do you treat them as currency to earn salvation or as expressions of a faith that already secures it? What would it cost you to stop keeping score?

The Mu'tazila Scriptural Proofs for Nullification
Author lists Mu'tazila scriptural proofs for nullification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic verses the Mu'tazila use to argue that major sins nullify good deeds.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila rely on three verses to argue that sins nullify good deeds.

The Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna disagreed about whether sins other than disbelief nullify good deeds. The Mu'tazila brought scriptural evidence from three Quranic verses. Let's examine their proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the dispute
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the disagreement between Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna on nullification by sins other than disbelief.
The passage begins with 'لا نزاع في إحباط الطاعات بالكفر' – there is no dispute about disbelief nullifying deeds.
2
Identify the three verses
The Mu'tazila cite three verses. Find each one and note which sin or action is linked to nullification.
The verses are from Surah al-Hujurat, at-Tawba, and al-Baqara.
3
Reflect on the apparent meaning
Think about how each verse might be interpreted to support nullification by a later sin. This prepares you for the response in the next lesson.
The next lesson will present the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ( في الإحباط لا نزاع في إحباط الطاعات بالكفر وأما بغيره فأثبته المعتزلة ونفاه أهل السنة، احتجت المعتزلة بقوله تعالى: ولا تجهروا له بالقول كجهر بعضكم لبعض أن تحبط أعمالكم [الحجرات: ٢]، وبقوله تعالى: أولئك حبطت أعمالهم [التوبة: ١٧]، وقوله تعالى: ولا تبطلوا صدقاتكم بالمن والأذى [البقرة: ٢٦٤]، أجاب في شرح المقاصد بأن من عمل عملاً يستحق به الذم مع إمكان عمله على وجه يستحق به المدح والثواب، يقال أنه أحبط عمله كالصدقة من المن والأذى وبدونهما وفيه نظر لأن الجواب إنما يتم إذا حمل آية الصدقة على مقارنة المن والأذى إذ لو تأخرا عنها وأبطلاها يثبت مدعاهم وذلك الحمل مخالف لظاهر النص ولا داعي لارتكابه، على أن قوله تعالى: إن الحسنات يذهبن السيئات [هود: ١١٤]، يدل على إبطال السيئة بالحسنة فالظاهر جواز عكسه أيضاً.
His statement: 'Regarding nullification, there is no dispute that obedience is nullified by disbelief, but regarding other than it, the Mu'tazila affirmed it and Ahl al-Sunna denied it. The Mu'tazila argued with the Almighty's saying: 'Do not speak loudly to him as you speak loudly to one another, lest your deeds become null' (49:2), and His saying: 'Those are the ones whose deeds have become null' (9:17), and His saying: 'Do not nullify your charities by reproach and injury' (2:264). He answered in Sharh al-Maqasid that one who does an action that deserves blame while it is possible to do it in a way that deserves praise is said to have nullified his action, like charity with reproach and injury and without them. But there is consideration in this because the answer is only complete if the verse of charity is interpreted as the simultaneity of reproach and injury; if they occurred after it and nullified it, their claim would be established, and that interpretation is contrary to the apparent meaning of the text and there is no need to adopt it. Moreover, the Almighty's saying: 'Indeed good deeds remove evil deeds' (11:114) indicates the nullification of evil by good, so it seems permissible also for the opposite.'
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage presents the scriptural arguments used by the Mu'tazila to prove that major sins nullify good deeds, and the response. The Mu'tazila cite three Quranic verses. The response from Sharh al-Maqasid argues that these verses refer to actions that are inherently blameworthy and could have been done in a praiseworthy manner, such as giving charity but accompanying it with reproach; the nullification is due to the fault in the action itself, not a later sin. However, this response is not fully satisfying because if reproach and injury occur after the charity, they could still nullify it (supporting the Mu'tazila), but that interpretation is forced. Additionally, verse 11:114 shows that good deeds remove evil deeds, suggesting it could work in reverse.

Key Terms
الإحباط
الآيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many verses do the Mu'tazila cite in this passage?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila cite three Quranic verses to argue that sins other than disbelief can nullify good deeds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila cite the verse about raising your voice above the Prophet. Do you treat the Prophet's commands with such reverence that you fear even a small act of disrespect could lose you your good deeds? How would you change your speech and behavior today to match that fear?
?
Read the verse about nullifying charity with reproach and injury. Have you ever given charity with a hint of pride or later reminded the recipient? If your good deeds can be voided by such actions, how will you guard your intentions and conduct from now on?
?
The Mu'tazila believe sins can cancel good deeds. Even if you hold the Sunni view, consider this: do you rely on your deeds enough to be concerned, or do you rely on Allah's mercy while ignoring the seriousness of your sins? What would it take for you to fear losing your deeds without losing hope in Allah?

Refuting the Mu'tazila on Nullification
Author presents response and critique of Mu'tazila scriptural proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the response to the Mu'tazila's scriptural argument and the author's critique of that response.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's proof fails because the verses refer to the nature of the action, not subsequent sins.

The Mu'tazila's proof using Quranic verses was met with a response from Sharh al-Maqasid, which argued that the nullification is due to the inherent flaw in the action. However, the author finds this response insufficient and offers his own critique, including a counter-argument from another verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the response from Sharh al-Maqasid
Read the passage that begins with 'أجاب في شرح المقاصد'. Understand the analogy of charity with reproach.
The response claims the nullification is because the action itself is blameworthy, not because a later sin nullifies it.
2
Understand the author's objection
The author points out that the response only works if reproach is simultaneous. If it comes later, the interpretation is forced and contrary to the apparent meaning.
The author says 'فيه نظر' meaning there is consideration/critique.
3
Note the counter-argument from verse 11:114
The author cites 'إِنَّ الْحَسَنَاتِ يُذْهِبْنَ السَّيِّئَاتِ' to argue that good deeds can nullify evil ones, suggesting the opposite might also be permissible.
This is an additional point, not a full proof, but it undermines the Mu'tazila's case.

Quantitative Consideration in Nullification
Author presents the Jubba'is' view on nullification based on magnitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jubba'is' doctrine that nullification of good deeds by sin depends on the magnitude of reward and punishment, not the number of deeds.

Core Idea
In divine reckoning, weight matters more than count.

The passage from al-Jurjani's commentary presents a key doctrine of the Jubba'is regarding how sins cancel out good deeds. They argue that it is not the number of acts of obedience versus sins that determines nullification, but the actual magnitude of their rewards and burdens. This subtle distinction has profound implications for understanding divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the Jubba'i position.
Notice the key terms الكثرة (quantity) and مقادير (magnitudes).
2
Identify the contrast
The passage explicitly contrasts 'numbers' (أعداد) with 'magnitudes' (مقادير). Understand why this distinction matters.
The example of a major sin outweighing many good deeds illustrates the principle.
3
Reflect on the limitation
The passage concludes that humans cannot determine this weighting; it is entrusted to Allah. Consider why this is a necessary conclusion.
This underscores human epistemic limits in matters of divine judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ( إلى رعاية الكثرة لا بالنظر إلى أعداد الطاعات والمعاصي بل بالنظر إلى مقادير الأجور والأوزار فرب كبيرة يغلب وزرها أجر طاعات كثيرة ولا سبيل إلى ضبط ذلك بل هو مفوض إلى علم الله تعالى.
His statement: 'Regarding the consideration of quantity, it is not with respect to the numbers of acts of obedience and sins, but with respect to the magnitudes of rewards and burdens. For a major sin may outweigh the reward of many acts of obedience. There is no way to determine that; rather it is entrusted to the knowledge of God Almighty.'
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage explains the view of al-Jubba'i and his son regarding the quantitative aspect of nullification. They held that the balance is not based on the number of good and bad deeds, but on the actual magnitudes of their rewards and punishments. A single major sin might outweigh the reward of many good deeds. However, it is impossible for humans to calculate this accurately; only God knows the precise weights.

Key Terms
الكثرة
المقادير
الأجور
الأوزار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that more good deeds always outweigh fewer sins, so sins cannot nullify good deeds if the number of good deeds is larger.
Correction: The Jubba'is teach that it is the magnitude (severity) of the sin, not the number of good deeds, that determines nullification. A single major sin can outweigh many good deeds.

Checkpoint

B) The magnitudes of rewards and burdens
C) The type of sin, not quantity
D) The intention behind the deeds

Key Takeaway
The Jubba'is teach that nullification depends on the magnitude of rewards and punishments, not on the number of deeds, and only Allah knows the precise measure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Scriptural Proofs for Nullification and Their Refutation
Presents Mu'tazila Quranic proofs and a counter-argument from Sharh al-Maqasid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila argued for nullification using Quranic verses, how the Ahl al-Sunna responded via Sharh al-Maqasid, and the key criticisms of that response.

Core Idea
The Quranic verses cited by the Mu'tazila do not decisively prove nullification of good deeds by minor sins.

In this passage, we encounter the scriptural debate on nullification (ihbat). The Mu'tazila present three Quranic verses as evidence, but the commentator from Sharh al-Maqasid offers a response that itself faces criticism. Understanding this exchange clarifies the Sunni position that minor sins do not nullify good deeds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the flow: proof, response, criticism, counter-point.
The passage is dense; focus on grasping the overall argument first.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila proof verses
Find the three Quranic verses the Mu'tazila cite. Understand how each is supposed to support nullification.
Verses 49:2, 9:17, and 2:264 are used.
3
Analyze the response and its flaw
Read the response from Sharh al-Maqasid and then the criticism of that response. Why is the interpretation of verse 2:264 problematic?
The key issue is whether the reproach/injury is simultaneous or subsequent.
4
Consider the final counter-point
The passage ends with a reference to verse 11:114. How does this verse relate to the debate? Does it support or undermine nullification?
The commentator suggests that if good deeds remove evil, the reverse might be apparent, but this is weak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( في الإحباط لا نزاع في إحباط الطاعات بالكفر وأما بغيره فأثبته المعتزلة ونفاه أهل السنة، احتجت المعتزلة بقوله تعالى : ولا تجهروا له بالقول كجهر بعضكم لبعض أن تحبط أعمالكم ﴾ [الحجرات : ٢]، وبقوله تعالى: أولئك حبطت أعمالهم ﴾ [التوبة : ١٧]، وقوله تعالى : ولا تبطلوا صدقاتكم بالمن والأذى ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٦٤] ، أجاب في شرح المقاصد بأن من عمل عملاً يستحق به الذم مع إمكان عمله على وجه يستحق به المدح والثواب، يقال أنه أحبط عمله كالصدقة من المن والأذى وبدونهما وفيه نظر لأن الجواب إنما يتم إذا حمل آية الصدقة على مقارنة المن والأذى إذ لو تأخرا عنها وأبطلاها يثبت مدعاهم وذلك الحمل مخالف لظاهر النص ولا داعي لارتكابه، على أن قوله تعالى : إن الحسنات يذهبن السيئات ) [ هود : ١١٤ ] ، يدل على إبطال السيئة بالحسنة فالظاهر جواز عكسه أيضاً.
His saying: Regarding nullification, there is no dispute that acts of obedience are nullified by disbelief. As for other than that, the Mu'tazila affirmed it and the Ahl al-Sunna denied it. The Mu'tazila argued by His statement: 'Do not raise your voices above the voice of the Prophet, lest your deeds become nullified' (49:2), and His statement: 'Those, their deeds have become nullified' (9:17), and His statement: 'Do not invalidate your charities by reproach and injury' (2:264). The commentator in Sharh al-Maqasid replied: One who performs an act deserving blame, when it is possible for him to perform it in a manner deserving praise and reward, it is said that he nullified his act, like charity with reproach and injury and without them. But this is questionable because the reply is only valid if the verse of charity is interpreted as the simultaneity of reproach and injury; if they occurred after it and invalidated it, that would prove their claim. This interpretation contradicts the apparent meaning of the text, and there is no need to adopt it. Moreover, His statement: 'Indeed good deeds remove evil deeds' (11:114) indicates the removal of evil by good, so the apparent is also the permissibility of its opposite.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (ihtijaj wa jawab) on the scriptural proofs for nullification. The Mu'tazila cite three Quranic verses: 49:2 (warning against raising voices, lest deeds become nullified), 9:17 (about polytheists whose deeds are nullified), and 2:264 (do not invalidate charity by reproach and injury). The commentator from Sharh al-Maqasid responds by interpreting these verses as referring to acts that are inherently blameworthy (e.g., charity done with pride), not that a later sin nullifies a prior good act. The objection to this response is that it only works if the reproach or injury is simultaneous with the charity; if they are separate, the verse would allow later nullification. But the reply is still problematic because it goes against the apparent meaning of the text. Furthermore, the verse 'good deeds remove evil deeds' (11:114) suggests that good deeds can cancel evil ones, so by analogy the reverse (evil cancelling good) might also be possible – but this is only a superficial inference.

Key Terms
حبط
أعمال
منّ
أذى
حسنات
سيئات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila proof from Quran is decisive and clearly establishes that minor sins nullify good deeds.
Correction: The response from Sharh al-Maqasid shows that the verses are not clear-cut; they require an interpretation that contradicts their apparent meaning, and even that interpretation does not fully support the Mu'tazila claim.

Checkpoint
What is the main criticism of the response from Sharh al-Maqasid?
A) It ignores the verses completely
B) It forces an interpretation of the charity verse that contradicts its apparent meaning
C) It admits that nullification is possible
D) It relies on a hadith

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila scriptural proofs for nullification are not straightforward; the Sunni response highlights interpretive issues, leaving the case for nullification unconvincing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you sometimes attach conditions or expect something in return? This lesson shows that charity can be nullified by reproach and injury if they accompany it. Examine your recent acts of kindness: did you harbor any sense of superiority or annoyance? Commit to purifying your intentions so that your good deeds remain intact.
?
The Mu'tazila feared that minor sins could wipe out good deeds, leading to despair. Ahl al-Sunna affirm that good deeds are not nullified by minor sins. Do you live with the constant fear that your sins undo your good deeds? How does this lesson's affirmation of Allah's mercy and the simultaneity condition change your confidence in your good works? Make a conscious effort today to trust that your good deeds are preserved unless you deliberately combine them with reproach.
?

In your daily life, do you rely more on your good deeds to cancel out sins, or do you seek forgiveness and repent? This lesson pushes you to recognize the weight of each action. Al-A'raf and Combined Reward and Punishment
al-Jurjani presents two possibilities for equal deeds: al-A'raf or combined reward and punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Jurjani presents two additional possibilities for those with equal deeds: residing on al-A'raf or experiencing a combination of reward and punishment, rejecting the necessity of purity.

Core Idea
Reward and punishment can be mixed, just as joy and grief coexist in worldly life.

In this lesson, we explore the third possibility for those whose good and bad deeds are equal. al-Jurjani presents two sub‑possibilities: first, that such a person may be from the people of al‑A'raf, as mentioned in a sound hadith; second, that reward and punishment may be combined, drawing an analogy to the simultaneous experience of joy and pain in this world. This challenges the notion that reward and punishment must be pure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the two possibilities.
The passage is short but dense; focus on the two scenarios.
2
Understand al-A'raf
Note that al-A'raf is a place between Paradise and Hell for those whose deeds are exactly balanced, supported by a sound hadith.
3
Examine the analogy
Consider the analogy of simultaneous grief and joy in worldly life as a proof that reward and punishment can be combined in the afterlife.
This is the core argument against requiring purity.
4
Reflect on the rejection of purity
Realize that al-Jurjani explicitly denies that purity (khulus) is a condition for the reality of reward and punishment.
This is a key doctrinal move.

( ويجوز ) أيضاً أن لا يثاب ولا يعاقب و لا يكون من أهل الجنة ولا النار بل ( يكون ) أي من استوت طاعاته ومعاصيه ) من أهل الأعراف كما ورد به الحديث الصحيح ويجوز أيضاً ) أن يجمع له بين الثواب والعقاب كما يرى أحدنا يدوم له. غمه ) من جهة ( وفرحه) من جهة أخرى (و) يدوم له ( ألمه ولذته كذلك لا يخلص له أحدهما ) في حيانه الدنيا ولا نسلم أن الخلوص معتبر في حقيقة الثواب والعقاب
And it is also permissible that he is not rewarded nor punished and is not from the people of Paradise nor the Fire, but rather he, meaning one whose acts of obedience and sins are equal, is from the people of al-A'raf as related in the authentic hadith. And it is also permissible that reward and punishment be combined for him, just as one of us sees his grief continues from one side and his joy from another side, and his pain and pleasure continue likewise, so neither is pure for him in his worldly life. And we do not accept that purity is considered in the reality of reward and punishment.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage presents two additional possibilities for the case where good and bad deeds are equal. First, the person may be neither rewarded nor punished but be among the people of al-A'raf (the heights between Paradise and Hell) as mentioned in a sound hadith. Second, it is possible that reward and punishment are combined, analogous to how a person experiences both grief and joy simultaneously in this world. The author concludes by rejecting the notion that purity (i.e., unmixed happiness or pain) is essential to the reality of reward and punishment.

Key Takeaway

The Sunni Doctrine of Intercession for Major Sinners
Confirms intercession for major sinners with hadith and Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Sunni doctrine holds the Prophet's intercession is specifically for major sinners to remove punishment, and the scriptural evidence for this position.

Core Idea
Intercession is for removing punishment from major sinners, not just for increasing reward.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif addresses one of the most distinctive Sunni beliefs about intercession. While all Muslims affirm that the Prophet will intercede on the Day of Judgment, the Sunnis specify that his intercession is for the people of major sins (ahl al-kaba'ir) to avert punishment. The author provides the consensus (ijma') and then cites a hadith and a Quranic verse, anticipating and refuting a potential objection from those who might limit the scope.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal English translation to get the overall meaning.
Pay attention to the structure: thesis, evidence, objection, response.
2
Identify the two pieces of evidence
The author gives two proofs: a hadith and a Quranic verse. Find each one and note how they are used to support intercession for major sinners.
The hadith is explicitly about 'ahl al-kaba'ir'. The verse is interpreted by context.
3
Understand the objection and its response
The passage includes an objection about the generality of 'sin' in the verse. Read how the author responds and why this strengthens the Sunni position.
This shows dialectical reasoning in kalam.
4
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this belief matters. How does it relate to mercy and hope for sinners? Also consider the difference from the Mu'tazila position mentioned.
Connects to broader theology of salvation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد التاسع : في شفاعة محمد أجمع الأمة على ثبوت أصل الشفاعة المقبولة له عليه الصلاة والسلام لكن هي عندنا لأهل الكبائر من الأمة في إسقاط العقاب عنهم لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : شفاعتي لأهل الكبائر من أمتي فإنه حديث صحيح ولقوله تعالى: {واستغفر لذنبك وللمؤمنين والمؤمنات} [محمد: 19] أي ولذنب المؤمنين لدلالة القرينة السابقة وهي ذكر الذنب وسيأتيك في بيان حقيقة الإيمان أن مرتكب الكبيرة مؤمن وطلب المغفرة لذنب المؤمن شفاعة له في إسقاط عقابه عنه. قوله: ( ولقوله تعالى: {واستغفر لذنبك وللمؤمنين والمؤمنات} ) قد يقال: هذا إنما يكون برهاناً إذا ثبت عموم الذنب للصغائر والكبائر بقرينة قوله: لذنبك فإن ذنبه صغير قطعاً فلا نعم نعم يكون إلزاماً للمعتزلة القائلين بعدم استحقاق العقاب بالصغائر حتى يحتاج إلى الشفاعة ويمكن أن يقال: لا شك في عموم الذنب للصغائر والكبائر ولا دليل على التخصيص، وأما قوله لذنبك فقد سبق أن معناه لذنب أمتك ولا يخفى عمومه للصغائر والكبائر على أن كون ذنبه له صغيراً لا تفيد تخصيص ذنب الأمة وهو ظاهر جداً.
The second observatory - the ninth objective: On the intercession of Muhammad. The community (umma) has agreed upon the affirmation of the principle of accepted intercession for him, peace be upon him, but it is with us for the people of major sins (ahl al-kaba'ir) of the community in removing punishment from them, due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'My intercession is for the people of major sins of my community' - for it is a sound hadith; and due to His saying, Most High: 'And ask forgiveness for your sin and for the believing men and believing women' [Muhammad 47:19], meaning for the sin of the believers, due to the indication of the previous context which is the mention of sin, and it will come to you in the explanation of the reality of faith that the committer of a major sin is a believer; and requesting forgiveness for the sin of the believer is intercession for him in removing punishment from him. His saying: (And due to His saying: 'And ask forgiveness for your sin and for the believing men and believing women') It may be said: This is only a proof if the generality of sin for minor and major sins is established by the context of His saying: 'for your sin' for his sin is certainly minor, so no, yes, yes it is an obligation against the Mu'tazila who say that minor sins do not deserve punishment so that intercession is needed. And it can be said: There is no doubt about the generality of sin for minor and major sins and there is no evidence for specification. As for His saying: 'for your sin,' it has already been stated that its meaning is 'for the sin of your umma' and its generality for minor and major sins is not hidden, and the fact that his sin is minor does not imply specification of the sin of the umma, and this is very clear.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage defines the Sunni position on intercession (shafa'a) and provides the primary evidence. It first states the consensus (ijma') of the community on the reality of intercession for the Prophet, then specifies that according to Sunnis it is for the people of major sins (ahl al-kaba'ir) to avert punishment. Two proofs are given: a hadith in which the Prophet says 'My intercession is for the people of major sins of my community' - declared a sound hadith - and the Quranic verse 47:19 where the Prophet is commanded to ask forgiveness for his own sin and for the believers. The verse is interpreted as asking forgiveness for the believers' sins, and the phrase 'for your sin' indicates the sin of the community by context. Then the text anticipates an objection: the verse might not prove intercession for major sins unless it is shown that 'sin' in the verse includes both minor and major. The objection argues that since the Prophet's own sin is only minor (as prophets are protected from major sins), the verse might only cover minor sins. The response (yumkin an yuqal) is that there is no doubt about the generality of sin (dhamb) for both minor and major, and the earlier interpretation that 'your sin' means 'the sin of your umma' already shows generality. The fact that the Prophet's sin is minor does not restrict the umma's sins. This defense solidifies the proof.

Correction: The passage explicitly states that intercession is for removing punishment (isqat al-'iqab) from major sinners.

D) The verse 'Yawma la tanfa'u shafa'atun...'

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's intercession is specifically for major sinners to remove punishment, as proven by the hadith and Quranic verse, defended against objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refutation of the Mu'tazilite Objection on Intercession
Responds to Mu'tazilite claim that intercession is only for increase of reward.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multi-layered argument against the Mu'tazilite claim that intercession is only for increase of reward, including responses based on generality, the discussion on indefinite nouns, and al-Razi's principle of prioritizing specific over general.

Core Idea
Intercession benefits even sinners; Mu'tazilite proofs are general but Sunni proofs are specific.

The Mu'tazila argued that intercession only increases reward, citing Quran 2:123 as evidence. This passage presents a nuanced refutation, first by showing the verse is not general, then addressing objections, and finally summarizing al-Razi's decisive argument that specific proofs outweigh general ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite claim and verse
Start by reading the Mu'tazilite position and the Quranic verse they cite (2:123). Understand their assertion that intercession is only for increase of reward.
The verse appears to deny intercession altogether, but the context is key.
2
Identify the first response: lack of generality
Notice the initial answer: the verse is not general in persons (it refers to Jews) nor in time (it refers to a specific Day). This limits its application.
This response opens a discussion about the pronoun's reference.
3
Understand the discussion on the pronoun
A discussion (bahth) follows: the pronoun in 'no intercession will benefit it' refers to an indefinite noun in negation, which would make it general. Reflect on how this is resolved.
The text later offers a resolution based on rational vs. linguistic generality.
4
Grasp al-Razi's summary and the principle
Finally, al-Razi summarizes that Mu'tazilite proofs must be general, while Sunni proofs are specific, and the specific takes precedence over the general. This settles the argument.
This is the core of the refutation: we do not claim intercession for everyone or all times.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة : إنما هي لزيادة الثواب لا لدرء العقاب لقوله تعالى : واتقوا يوماً لا تجزى نفس عن نفس شيئاً ولا يقبل منها عدل ولا تنفعها شفاعة ﴾ [البقرة : ۱۲۳ ]، وهو عام في شفاعة النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام وغيره الجواب أنه لا عموم له في الأعيان لأن الضمير لقوله معينين هم اليهود فلا يلزم أن لا تنفع الشفاعة غيرهم ولا عموم له في الزمان أيضاً لأنه لوقت مخصوص هو اليوم المذكور فيه فلا يلزم عدم نفعها فيغير ذلك الوقت وفيه بحث لأن الضمير في قوله ولا تنفعها راجع إلى النفس الثانية وهي نكرة في سياق النفي فتكون عامة قوله : (إنما هي لزيادة الثواب) اعترض عليه بأن الشفاعة لو كانت حقيقة فيما ذكروا لكنا شافعين للنبي عليه السلام حيث نسأل الله تعالى زيادة كرامته وهو باطل اتفاقاً، أجيب بجواز أن يعتبر في الشفاعة كون الشفيع أعلى من المشفع، وأما القول بأن الشفيع قد يشفع لنفسه ولا يكون أعلى ففيه أنه على سبيل الشبه والمجاز، إذ الشفاعة إنما تطلق في العرف على دعاء الرجل لغيره كما يدل عليه اشتقاقه من الشفع كان المشفوع له فرد يجعله الشفيع شفعاً بضم نفسه إليه . قوله : ( وهو عام إلخ ) فيه بحث إذ لو أبقى ما يفهم من ظاهر الآية على عمومه كما زعموه لزم أن لا يثبت الشفاعة أصلا لأن زيادة الثواب نفع عظيم، فإذا خصص فالأمر سهل . قوله : ( وفيه بحث لأن الضمير إلخ ) قد يجاب عنه بأنه لا ضرورة في رجوع الضمير إليها من حيث عمومها فإن النكرة المنفية خاصة بحسب الوضع وعمومها عقلي ضروري كما تقرر النفي فتكون عامة وإن كانت واردة على سبب خاص، والإمام الرازي بعدما أورد شبهات المعتزلة في إثبات ما ادعوه قال : والجواب عنها إجمالاً أن يقال : دلائلكم في نفي الشفاعة لا بد أن تكون عامة في الأشخاص والأوقات ودلائلنا في إثباتها لا بد أن تكون خاصة فيهما لأنا لا نثبت الشفاعة في حق كل شخص ولا في جميع الأوقات والخاص مقدم على العام فالترجيح معنا وأما الأجوبة المفصلة فمذكورة في التفسير الكبير.
The Mu'tazila said: Intercession is only for increase of reward, not for averting punishment, due to His saying, Most High: 'And fear a Day when no soul will suffice for another soul at all, and no compensation will be accepted from it, and no intercession will benefit it' [Quran 2:123], and it is general concerning the intercession of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and others. The answer: It has no generality with respect to persons, because the pronoun refers to specific people (namely the Jews), so it does not necessarily follow that intercession will not benefit others; and it also has no generality with respect to time, because it is for a specific time (the mentioned Day), so it does not necessarily follow that it will not benefit at other times. There is a discussion (bahth) because the pronoun in 'nor will intercession benefit it' refers to the second soul, which is an indefinite noun in the context of negation, so it is general. His saying: (It is only for increase of reward) An objection was raised against it: If intercession were real as they claim, we would be intercessors for the Prophet, peace be upon him, when we ask God for increase of his honor, and that is false by consensus. It was answered: It is possible that in intercession, the intercessor must be higher than the one for whom intercession is made. As for the saying that the intercessor may intercede for himself and is not higher, it is on the basis of similarity and metaphor, because intercession in custom is only applied to the supplication of a man for another, as its derivation from 'shaf'' indicates: the one for whom intercession is made is an individual, and the intercessor makes him a pair by joining himself to him. His saying: (And it is general, etc.) There is a discussion: If what is understood from the apparent meaning of the verse were left upon its generality as they claim, it would necessitate that intercession is not established at all, because increase of reward is a great benefit, so when it is specified, the matter is easy. His saying: (And there is a discussion because the pronoun, etc.) It may be answered: There is no necessity for the pronoun to refer to it in terms of its generality, because the negated indefinite is specific according to its linguistic form, and its generality is a necessary rational matter as established, so the negation makes it general even if it occurred due to a specific cause. Imam al-Razi, after presenting the doubts of the Mu'tazila in proving what they claimed, said: The answer to them in summary is that your proofs in negating intercession must be general in persons and times, while our proofs in affirming it must be specific in both, for we do not affirm intercession for every person nor in all times, and the specific is given precedence over the general, so the weight is with us. As for the detailed answers, they are mentioned in the Great Commentary (al-Tafsir al-Kabir).
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite position that intercession is only for increase of reward (ziyadat al-thawab) and not for averting punishment. The Mu'tazila cite Quran 2:123, which says no intercession will benefit on a certain Day, claiming it is general. The first response argues that the verse is not general in persons (it refers specifically to the Jews) nor in time (it refers to a specific Day). However, a discussion (bahth) follows: the pronoun 'it' in 'nor will intercession benefit it' refers to an indefinite noun in a negative context, which would make it general. The text then addresses another objection: if intercession were for increase of reward, then we would be intercessors for the Prophet by asking God to increase his honor, which is false. The answer distinguishes that the intercessor must be higher than the one for whom he intercedes. Next, a further discussion considers that if the verse were left general, it would negate intercession entirely, which is absurd because even increase of reward is a benefit. The commentator then resolves the issue by stating that a negated indefinite is specific in linguistic form but its generality is rational, and it can be specified. Finally, Imam al-Razi's summary is quoted: the Mu'tazilite proofs must be general, while Sunni proofs are specific, and the specific is given precedence over the general, so the Sunni position is superior. Detailed arguments are in al-Razi's Great Commentary.

Key Terms
زيادة الثواب
درء العقاب
عموم
خاص
شفاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Quran 2:123 completely negates intercession for all people and all times.
Correction: The verse is limited to specific persons (the Jews) and a specific time (the Day of Judgment), and does not negate intercession in general.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument used by Imam al-Razi to refute the Mu'tazilite position?
A) The Mu'tazilite proofs are specific while Sunni proofs are general
B) The Mu'tazilite proofs are general while Sunni proofs are specific
C) The Qur'an verses are all general and cannot be specified
D) Intercession is only for the Prophet himself

Key Takeaway
Intercession is affirmed by specific proofs that outweigh general verses; it is not negated for all people or times.

The Principle of Specific over General in Intercession
Discussion of preference for specific proofs over general in intercession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle of preferring specific evidence over general evidence supports the Sunni position on intercession.

Core Idea
Specific proofs outweigh general proofs in the debate on intercession.

This passage presents a key methodological argument from Imam al-Razi. When debating intercession, the Mu'tazilites used general proofs to deny it, while the Sunnis used specific proofs to affirm it. The principle of usul al-fiqh that the specific takes precedence over the general gives the Sunni position logical strength. Let's explore this concise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice that the passage includes a direct quote from Imam al-Razi.
2
Identify the two types of proofs
The passage contrasts the Mu'tazilite proofs (general in persons and times) with the Sunni proofs (specific in both). Underline or note these descriptions.
The terms 'عام' (general) and 'خاص' (specific) are key.
3
Understand the rule of precedence
Al-Razi states that 'الخاص مقدم على العام' (the specific is given precedence over the general). Reflect on why this rule would make the Sunni argument stronger.
This is a well-known principle in usul al-fiqh.
4
Consider the implication
Because the Sunni proofs are specific and the Mu'tazilite proofs are general, the specific outweighs the general, so the preponderance (الترجيح) is with the Sunnis.
This does not mean intercession is absolutely universal; it is limited to certain persons and times.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النفي فتكون عامة وإن كانت واردة على سبب خاص، والإمام الرازي بعدما أورد شبهات المعتزلة في إثبات ما ادعوه قال : والجواب عنها إجمالاً أن يقال : دلائلكم في نفي الشفاعة لا بد أن تكون عامة في الأشخاص والأوقات ودلائلنا في إثباتها لا بد أن تكون خاصة فيهما لأنا لا نثبت الشفاعة في حق كل شخص ولا في جميع الأوقات والخاص مقدم على العام فالترجيح معنا وأما الأجوبة المفصلة فمذكورة في التفسير الكبير.
The negation is general even if it is based on a specific cause. Imam al-Razi, after presenting the doubts of the Mu'tazilites in proving what they claimed, said: The answer to them in summary is to say: your proofs for the negation of intercession (al-shafa'a) must be general with respect to persons and times, while our proofs for its affirmation must be specific with respect to them, because we do not affirm intercession for every person nor at all times. The specific (al-khass) is given precedence over the general (al-'amm), so the preponderance is with us. As for detailed answers, they are mentioned in al-Tafsir al-Kabir.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage presents a summary argument for the Sunni position on intercession (al-shafa'a). It begins by stating a rule: a general negation (from a specific cause) remains general. Then it quotes Imam al-Razi's approach in refuting the Mu'tazilite arguments against intercession. The method is to note that the opponents' proofs for denying intercession are general in scope (applying to all persons and times), whereas the Sunni proofs for affirming intercession are specific (limited to certain persons and times). Since specific evidence takes precedence over general evidence, the Sunni position is stronger. This illustrates an important principle in usul al-fiqh: the specific is given precedence over the general when they conflict. The passage also refers to detailed answers in al-Razi's Tafsir al-Kabir.

Key Terms
الشفاعة
النفي
العام
الخاص
الترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That intercession is absolutely universal or universally denied
Correction: The passage clarifies that intercession is specific to certain persons and times, not universal in scope.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi, why is the Sunni argument for intercession stronger than the Mu'tazilite argument?
A) Because the Sunnis have more evidence
B) Because specific evidence outweighs general evidence
C) Because the Mu'tazilites ignore tradition
D) Because intercession is only for sinners

Key Takeaway
The Sunni argument for intercession is stronger because it relies on specific evidence, which takes precedence over the general evidence of the Mu'tazilites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a general statement about Islam (e.g., 'All sinners will be punished'), do you automatically accept it, or do you seek the specific qualifications and exceptions revealed in the sources? Do you have the courage to hold both the general and the specific in tension, or do you prefer the simplicity of the absolute?
?
Think of a time when you judged someone's spiritual state based on a general rule without considering the specific circumstances or divine mercy. How might al-Razi's principle of specific over general transform your attitude toward others' shortcomings?
?
You know that intercession is specific, not universal. Does that make you complacent or more earnest in seeking the means to be among those included? Are you actively pursuing the specific conditions that would make intercession apply to you, or are you relying on a vague hope?

The Revival of the Dead in Graves: A Core Belief of Ahl al-Haqq
The author states the belief in revival, questioning, and punishment in the grave as true.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the doctrine of revival in the grave, the questioning by Munkar and Nakir, and the punishment of the grave as affirmed by Ahl al-Haqq, including the nuanced position on the soul's return.

Core Idea
Grave revival is a partial life for reward and punishment, not requiring the soul's full return.

This passage presents the eleventh observation on eschatological matters. The author affirms that revival, questioning, and punishment in the grave are true, agreed upon by the early community and the majority. However, a key nuance emerges: the life in the grave is partial, sufficient for experiencing pain and pleasure, and Ahl al-Haqq hesitate about the return of the soul itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation, noting the three components: revival, Munkar and Nakir questioning, and grave punishment.
The passage uses strong language: 'all are true according to us'.
2
Understand the consensus
Notice that the author emphasizes agreement by the Salaf and the majority, implying these doctrines are well-established.
3
Examine the nuance on the soul
The passage explains that Ahl al-Haqq hesitate (tawaqqafū) about the soul's return. They deny that life is impossible without the soul in this context.
This is a key distinctive point.
4
Reflect on the partial life
Understand that grave life is a type of life (nawʿ ḥayāt) for feeling pain and pleasure, not complete life with voluntary actions.
The passage ends with 'but it is problematic' indicating further discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الحادي عشر : إحياء الموتى في القبور. إحياء الموتى في قبورهم ومسألة منكر ونكير لهم وعذاب القبر للكافر والفاسق كلها حق عندنا واتفق عليه سلف الأمة قبل ظهور الخلاف واتفق عليه الأكثر. قوله : إحياء الموتى في قبورهم اتفق أهل الحق على أن الله تعالى يعيد إلى الميت في القبر نوع حياة قدر ما يتألم ويتلذذ لكن توقفوا في عود الروح وما يتوهم من امتناع الحياة بدون الروح ممنوع ، وإنما ذلك في الحياة الكاملة التي معها القدرة على الأفعال الاختيارية لكن يشكل
The Eleventh Observation: The Revival of the Dead in Graves. The revival of the dead in their graves, the question of Munkar and Nakir for them, and the punishment of the grave for the disbeliever and the sinner – all of these are true according to us, and the predecessors of the community agreed upon before the emergence of disagreement, and the majority agreed upon it. His statement: 'The revival of the dead in their graves' – Ahl al-Haqq agreed that God restores to the dead in the grave a type of life to the extent of feeling pain and pleasure, but they hesitated regarding the return of the soul. What is imagined of the impossibility of life without the soul is denied. Rather, that (dependence on the soul) applies only to complete life that includes ability for voluntary actions, but it is problematic...
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage addresses the eleventh observation: revival of the dead in graves. The author affirms that revival, the questioning by Munkar and Nakir, and grave punishment for disbelievers and sinners are true, agreed upon by the salaf and the majority. A commentary explains that Ahl al-Haqq agree on a partial life for experiencing pain/pleasure but disagree on the return of the soul. They deny that life is impossible without the soul; that only holds for full life with voluntary actions.

عذاب القبر (ʿadhāb al-qabr)
روح (rūḥ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Revival in the grave requires the full return of the soul for voluntary actions.
Correction: Ahl al-Haqq affirm a partial life without the full return of the soul, sufficient only for experiencing pain and pleasure.

Checkpoint

A) They affirm the soul returns fully.
B) They deny any life without the soul.
C) They hesitate about the soul's return.
D) They consider grave life impossible.

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Haqq affirm a partial life in the grave for reward and punishment, without requiring the soul's full return for voluntary actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that your grave will soon be a place of either pleasure or pain based on your deeds, would you still waste time on sinful habits? What one habit will you commit to change starting today?
?
The author mentions that the early community agreed on these matters before any doubt arose. Are you personally convinced of the unseen realities of the grave, or do you harbour hidden doubts? How can you strengthen your certainty?
?
The partial life in the grave means you will feel every consequence of your actions, even without full consciousness. Does this thought make you fear Allah more or merely intellectualize the belief? What will you do today to make this belief a living reality in your heart?
